ABSTRACT 
This research used a theoretical model that included six decision-making styles to analyse the 
decision-making styles of executives in Qatar. Quantitative research techniques were used in this study, 
but these were supplemented with semi-structured interviews conducted with four top executive 
decision makers to provide an indication of decision-making in Qatari Executives, the covid 19 
pandemic obstructed further interviews and detail thematic analysis. However, a five-point Likert scale 
questionnaire was custom designed that contained six decision-making styles of the theoretical model 
that were identified from existing theory and empirical research: rational, heuristic, intuitive, 
dependent, avoidant, and spontaneous decision-making styles. A series of eleven dichotomous 
questions represented decision-making contexts at different levels and there were four demographic 
variables. The questionnaire was administered to 350 Qatari executives. Principal components analysis 
indicated that all the six decision-making styles were important to the Qatari executives with different 
level of agreement.  
The research findings confirm that decision-making is a multifaceted and multi-dimensional 
phenomenon, where the focus in decision-making varies from context to context: Qatari executives 
use a combination of decision-making styles in making important decisions. However, the research 
indicates that individuals, at times rely on shortcuts when making decisions.  A mixed environment 
driven model to decision-making process is proposed. In this model, when problems that are more 
complex, and dynamic are coupled with a poor environment then more intuitive and heuristic processes 
are used and less rational. That contrasts with situations involving simple, stable scenarios that are 
coupled with a supportive environment where more rational and risk avoidant process are used, using 
less intuition and fewer heuristics.  
Age was not significantly correlated with any of the decision-making styles. Avoidant 
decision-making style had a positive significant correlation with number of years in the current 
business. Intuitive decision-making style had a positive significant correlation with level of Education 
and dependent decision-making style has positive significant correlation with both number of years in 
the current business and level of education. Spontaneous decision-making was not dependent on any 
of the eleven business contexts.  
Concerning different business contexts, immediate payment decisions slightly negatively 
impacted on all the six decision-making styles but not significantly. Avoidant decision-making style 
was negatively impacted by the day-to-day activities of the business and negatively impacted by short-
term activities but positively impacted by long-term activities. Heuristic decision-making was 
impacted positively by short-term activities but negatively by the allocation of resources, the use of 
resources, how many people to deploy on the task, broad organisational issues, top executives, and 
strategic decisions. Rational decision making was significantly impacted on by the use of resources 
and how many people are deployed on tasks. Intuitive decision making was significantly impacted 
negative by short-term activities. Dependent decision-making was impacted negatively by how many 
people to deploy on tasks and broad organisational issues. Two of the eleven business concerns a) 
immediate payment decisions and b) managers across the organisation do not significantly impact on 
any of the decision-making styles.  
Keywords: Decision Making Process, Decision Making Styles, Factor Analysis, Heuristic Decision 
Making, Rational Decision Making, Intuitive Decision Making, Dependent Decision Making, 
Spontaneous Decision Making, Avoidant Decision Making. 
CHAPTER ONE:  INTRODUCTION  
1.1 RESEARCH OVERVIEW 
Decision making theory primarily addresses how situational organisational structures 
impact decisions. According to decision making theorists, decision making is not centred around 
efficiency; things are done in particular ways because that is how it was done in the past. 
Contradictions and irrationality are prevalent within organisations. Decision makers are limited 
because of their perceptions and inability to adequately obtain and assess information (March & 
Simon, 1958). Perceptions are bias and bounded by sense-making determined by previous 
experience and knowledge (Weick, 1970). For this reason, organisational history is vital to 
understanding the behaviour of individuals within organisations.  
From the decision-making perspective, organisational structure is fluid, as it must be 
enacted each moment as individuals must continuously perceive and make sense of the world 
around them. However, decision-making is limited due to bounded rationality (limits in 
information and constraints on individuals to adequately assess information). According to 
decision making theory, organisations are made up of a mix of goals, problems, and possible 
solutions (Levitt & Nass, 1989). To ensure routine behaviour in organisations, programs are 
developed. Programs are routinised activities in response to frequently experienced stimuli. 
Programs facilitate yet limit worker understanding. Programs increase understanding, as they 
evoke meaning to common experiences. However, programs limit understanding, as they focus 
attention to some events, while ignoring others. Organisations affect individual rationality through 
programs which influence individual attention and subgoals along with the division of labour and 
communication structures.   
Whereas scientific management and human relations focus on managerial decisions to 
increase efficiency, decision making theory focuses on effectiveness. Decision making theorists 
claim decisions are usually determined by effectiveness, not efficiency (March and Simon, 1958). 
Individuals tend to routinise their behaviour and develop programs in response to stimuli.  It is 
costly for actors to have efficiency involved when making decisions. Instead, actors tend to think 
of effectiveness when completing tasks- they just worry about getting the job done. 
Decision making theory strongly contrasts with both the scientific management and human 
relations theory. Decision making theory strongly criticizes the managerial bias, as it claims it is 
not possible for managers to control the work process. From this perspective, control systems are 
situated (Vaughn, 1998). Control systems emerge because of past decisions and others 
interpretation of responses, they cannot be determined by current management. All individuals in 
the organisation, including managers, face bounded rationality. As such, everyone within the 
organisation is constrained in their ability to adequately assess information and control 
organisational outcomes.  
Decision making theory builds from formal rational perspectives of organisations, but 
according to decision making theory, formal rationality only has half of the equation. Whereas 
formal rationality emphasises how organisational control is ensured through a focus of 
information, decision making theory emphasises how both the focus of information and the focus 
of attention is related to organisational control (March and Simon, 1958). Historically developed 
control systems (programs) influence the way individuals decide when and how to act when 
experiencing stimuli. In conclusion, according to decision making theory, organisations work as 
sets of continuously enacted programmed responses, allowing worker discretion and flexibility in 
how a mix of goals is achieved.  
Most of economic and business firms meet daily set of structural and unstructured problems 
which need managers with high skills and capabilities to make solutions through decisions 
(Francis, 2016; Radaoui et al., 2019). Structural decisions are defined as simple or routine 
decisions, but the problem lies on the unstructured decisions which the success or failure of firms 
depends on (Radaoui et al., 2019). In additions, the development process within the firm must go 
on in the goal of taking decisions that are able to find solutions necessary to solve all problems 
and issues. 
Developing the decision-making process is considered a development for business 
processes and functions, because it is difficult to perform any activity without having a good 
decision maker (Francis, 2016) and decision-making may be undertaken by one individual, or a 
group of individuals where different opinions are needed (Ogarca, 2015). 
In approaching the organisational decision-making processes, the managers behave 
differently. These differences are due to the specific way of framing the decisional problems, 
perceiving and analysing the available information, experience, interacting with the subordinates 
in the decision-making process. Furthermore, when approaching the management processes, there 
are sensitive differences between entrepreneurs and the managers of the large companies (Ogarca, 
2015). 
The organisation with flat structure which give the ability for employees to enter in the 
decision-making process is considered more effective than the hierarchical organisation which 
only the executive manager is making decisions (Adam et al., 2018). for that, most of firms started 
to adopt the concept of empowerment, which means expanding the circle of participants of internal 
customers (employees) and external customers to have innovative ideas and solutions and to have 
more experience to support decision-making with more efficient results even if there is a 
disadvantage of needing more time to decide. 
Over the past 25 years, as some senior executives of several organisations, they noticed 
that the decision-making process they have applied was often based on personal experience. 
Discussing this decision-making process with their colleagues possessing similar organisation 
backgrounds confirms that most organisation executives they talked with rely on personal 
experience when solving challenging organisation problems. Of course, they could not always 
share with others, especially the competition, the specifics of the problems facing their 
organisation (Frankl, 2010).  
Nowadays the role of senior executives includes a large amount of decision-making and 
complex problem solving. The ability of organisations to take efficient and effective decisions can 
have a major impact on business sustainability and agility (Forrester, 2003). Organisations realise 
the significance positive impact of developing true decision competencies and only realise this 
when solid, high-quality decision-making becomes embedded in the fabric of their DNA (Spetzler, 
2007). 
Decision literature depicts that past researchers introduced numerous unique decision-
making styles according to their cultures, contexts, and circumstances, but the shared aspect of the 
research on decision making styles is the consideration of rational and intuitive decision making  
style by almost all of the decision researchers (Arroba, 1977; Harren, 1979; Mitroff & Kilmann, 1975; 
Phillips et al., 1984; Scott & Bruce, 1995) although they considered both styles as distinctive 
dimensions of human nature. Researchers (Klaczynski, 2001; Stanovich & West, 2000; Sternberg, 
1997) illustrated that the line of distinction between cognitive style, learning style, thinking style, 
and decision-making style is based on the distinction between rational and intuitive approaches. 
The existing literature indicates that decision researchers were stick to the assumption that 
decisions are solely based either on rationality or on intuition. Payne & Bettman (2004) suggested 
that attempts should be made to understand the interaction between these two styles instead of 
considering them as separate dimensions to confirm Stanovich & West, (2002) argument that these 
two styles do not stand apart on the poles and are more interactive in nature. Francis (2016) defined 
four decision making models: the rational, intuitive decision, dependent decision, and avoidant 
decision models. 
In this research the rational model will be taking into consideration. The rational model is 
an extremely important model, since it is publicly prescribed to and because it is often the target 
of those who support alternative models, it is simple to overlook that it is merely a model, an 
incomplete representation of what occurs (AlHussayen, 2009). Researchers often measure 
decisions and inferred processes. A long-standing assumption within the decision-making 
literature is that the deliberative mode results in good (unbiased, rational) decisions (Peters et 
al., 2010; Strough et al., 2011). However, heuristics such as avoid waste often yield effective 
solutions and require minimal time and cognitive effort they are fast and frugal. 
This research analyses the decision-making styles, including rational and heuristic 
elements, in executive decision-making process in Qatar. The research studies how heuristic and 
rational, and other approaches to decision-making vary according to different strategic decision-
making contexts. Measures of rationality drawn from the literature are used in conjunction with 
techniques of argument mapping to represent decisions in a standardized manner. The concept of 
the strategic level of a decision will be investigated and a scale constructed for its assessment 
drawing on factors in the literature and a survey of managers understanding of the concept of 
strategic level.  
A structured questionnaire has been custom designed and completed by 350 Qatari 
Executive Managers from public sector and various business areas and semi-structured interviews 
were conducted with a subset of four Qatari senior managers. All four managers that participated 
in the semi-structured questionnaire are at CEO, divisional head, or similar senior positions level. 
1.2 RESEARCH CONTEXT 
Management, in both private and public sector, is the major component to increase the 
capacity of economy to improve its performance, and cope with continuous technological changes. 
In todays world, organisations are faced with thousands of decisions daily, and how they make 
these decisions will have a huge impact on their financial status (Forrester, 2003). These decisions 
set the tone for the entire organisation in terms of image, profits and customer service. That is why, 
it is especially important that organisations adopt best practices and execute good judgment when 
it comes to making decisions. Because the right decision at the right time could help companies 
achieve great success whereas a wrong decision could end up costing them dearly (Gal et al., 
2013). 
Frankl (2010) argued that wide varieties of human endeavours use heuristics for problem-
solving and noticed that his decision-making processes were often based either on experience or 
on advice received from colleagues. Seldom were the decisions based on formal or informal 
academic-based methods. He argued that there is no substitute for years of experience in any 
human endeavour. However, tapping into some of the principles and lessons learned from most of 
the senior executives personal experience can result in useful methods for others to follow, 
especially for entrepreneurs interested in building their own businesses. Therefore, a method for 
organising and compiling various forms of decision-making methods could deliver significant 
benefits by improving the decision-making process of executives in their institution. 
Decision making plays a vital and crucial role within large organisations, and how they 
react could very well have a substantial impact on their financial standing. Failure to make the 
correct decision could lead to huge financial loss, while on the other hand making the right decision 
could help achieve a financial gain. The importance of the correct decision being made cannot be 
signified. Therefore, and to guarantee success and survival in intensifying competition, all factors 
affecting decision-making need to be considered when deciding on a course of action 
(AlHussayen, 2009). 
The rational model suffers from several limitations like not having enough information 
which is crucial and relevant to problem, problems can also change during short period of time. In 
reality, the goals of an organisation are not the same of those of complex groups working in it. As 
there are different individuals in affirm, they have their own views, desires and objectives which 
might not be relevant and crucial for an organisation. In fact, they can work against the firm; those 
individuals will try to maximize their own set of goals rather than those which hold relevance for 
the firm (Verma, 2014). 
Although decision-making process has been a subject of research in big corporations, 
investigating decision-making using heuristics element remains limited. A method for organising 
and compiling various elements of decision-making using different elements of heuristics and 
rational can deliver significant benefits to during decision-making process. Behavioural 
economists acknowledge that decisions are often irrational and subject to systematic biases 
(Strough et al., 2011). However, they have not addressed whether the heuristics and rational 
element will improve the decision-making process.  
Qatars economy is one of the richest economies in the world based on GDP per capital, 
ranking between fifth and seventh on world rankings for 2015 and 2016 data compiled by the 
World Bank, United Nations, and IMF. Petroleum and natural gas are the cornerstones of Qatars 
economy and account for more than 70% of total government revenue, more than 60% of gross 
domestic product, and roughly 85% of export earnings. Qatar has the worlds third largest proven 
natural gas reserve and is the second-largest exporter of natural gas. 
Despite turbulent oil prices in the couple of last years the countrys economic growth 
remained stable. Government efforts to diversify incomes and globally invest reserves supported 
the GDP. The economy is also showing resilience and despite the ongoing blockade had an 
estimated growth of 2.6% in 2017. Sustaining economic prosperity is a strategic goal for Qatar in 
2030 and effective and efficient decision making will support achievement of that goal. Qatar 
National Vision 2030 (2022) presents three interrelated goals for the economy: to sustain a high 
standard of living, to expand innovation and entrepreneurial capabilities and to align economic 
outcomes with economic and financial stability. 
The population of Qatar is also on a rise by 2.1% from 2017, reaching 2.7 million people, 
more than doubling in the last ten years. It is a young population with an average age of 31 years 
coming from 87 nationalities. Only 12% of the population are local Qatari, while more than a third 
is coming from India and Nepal. People from Bangladesh and Philippines are also numerous with 
around 10% of the population each. 
Individuals seem to make decisions using a wide variety of strategies, ranging from careful 
and reasoned examination of alternatives to simple and fast rules of thumb. How do people decide 
how to decide? What factors determine the strategy a decision-maker will use in one situation as 
opposed to another? Answers to such questions are crucial for building a theoretical and empirical 
account of Decision-making. The challenge Qatari executives face is that of making better 
decisions. Currently manager executives make decisions using gut feel, common sense, intuition, 
experience, and, often without knowing it, various forms of heuristics or rules of thumb, and some 
time various form of rational element.  
Therefore, this thesis is an in-depth study of decision-making process, it assesses decision-
making elements (heuristics and rational) across Qatari executive managers and attempts to 
formulate guidelines for improving these elements. Although the empirical evidence is drawn from 
Qatar specific result, this study can be applied to other countries, individuals, and any decision-
making processes. Current research in the field has only explored elements of good decision 
quality, and smart organisations in contrast extraordinarily little guidance is available for 
organisations to improve their decision-making abilities. This research identifies different 
decision-making styles and develops a model that businesses can use to improve their decision-
making capabilities. 
1.3 THE IMPORTANCE OF STUDYING MANAGEMENT 
One of the essential indicators, of the development in any economy is management. There 
is increasing awareness about the impact of management on economic growth, the quality of 
management, and the adoption of appropriate management practices, matters and directly impacts 
economic growth (Keith, 2014) ), who argues that a review of the literature indicates that the 
contribution of management to economic growth has been largely obscured in theory and ignored 
in empirical work), he continues (Routine innovation in established firms is emphasised as a 
fundamental, normal part of management activity, and as such highlights the importance of 
management for economic growth, management is not homogeneous, and the actions of managers 
differentially affect the performance outcomes of firms). 
On the other hand, many significant contributions within recent theoretical debate about 
the differences between developing and developed economies; acknowledge that the distinction 
between them becomes less clear. The contrast between them, is not as clear as it was in the early 
post WWII times. Experts in the United Nations specialized agencies, start revising the classical 
approach, and its specialized team keep proposing new indicators, one of them is the model of 
management in private and public sectors.  
The rapid and continuous changes in many economies, creates theoretical and practical 
difficulties in differentiating between developed and developing countries. Some indicators were 
self-sufficient and valid few decades ago, becomes outdated by the fact that, the phenomenon 
weighted by these indicators has changed. However, the gap between developed and developing 
economy, measured in some new indicators is widening. 
Qatar is a country of obscured contrast between developed and developing economies. 
Which in many indicators it is a developed economy, and in some others, it is still far behind, and 
belongs to the developing world. One of the factors, which might refer to the possibilities and 
speed of development, is the gap between old rules of practice, and the new requirements to adapt 
with the recent technological change. It is easy to see in Qatar recent technology, concurrent with 
old modes of management and administration. 
1.4 RESEARCH QUESTIONS 
Based upon the literature review on decision-making styles, this study extends what Scott 
and Bruce (1995) have used during their research investigations and consequently the research 
explores different decision-making styles, including rational and heuristic elements, in executive 
decision-making process in Qatar. Hence, the following research questions are proposed which are 
linked to the research objectives: 
 RQ1  How could the use of rational and heuristic elements be used effectively in decision-
making in Qatar? (O1, O4 and Chapter 2  Literature Survey) 
 RQ2  What in practice is the use of different decision-making styles by executives in 
decision-making? (O1, O2, O3 and Chapter 3  Literature Survey and Questionnaire). 
 RQ3- What is the relation between the rational and heuristic decision-making styles with 
executive decision-making processes? (O2, O3 and Chapter 3  Literature Survey and 
Research Questionnaire). 
 RQ4  What decision-making styles profiles do executive decision-makers in Qatar have? 
(O2, O3 and Research Questionnaire) 
 RQ5  What recommendations can be made for improving the use of decision-making 
styles by executive decision makers in Qatar? (O4 and Chapter 5  Questionnaire). 
1.5 THE AIM OF THE STUDY 
The aim of this research is to analyse in detail the decision-making processes and the use 
of rational and heuristics elements in executives decision-making processes in Qatar. The research 
answers several specific questions, related to different decision-making styes which have not yet 
been answered by the presently available literature in general and specifically in the Qatari context. 
1.6 RESEARCH OBJECTIVES 
The objectives of this study are as follows and are connected to the research questions: 
O1: To investigate the managers awareness and understanding of the applied mix of decision-
making styles, including rational and heuristic styles, in executive decision-making in Qatar (RQ1 
and RQ2),  
O2: To investigate the relationship of decision-making styles with executives strategic contexts 
(RQ2 and RQ3),  
O3: To investigate the relationship of decision-making styles with executives demographics (RQ2 
and RQ3), 
O4: To suggest the direction of improvement, derived from relevant academic research, by which 
executives' decision-making should follow (RQ5). 
Accordingly, the following overall proposition and two hypotheses are presented: - 
The main proposition: 
P1 Executives in Qatar use a range of decision-making styles, including rational and heuristic 
styles, that can be profiled using a structured questionnaire. 
Hypotheses: 
H01: There is NO Statistically Significant (0.05) difference between decision-making styles of 
executives and their strategic contexts in Qatar. 
H02: There is NO Statistically Significant (0.05) difference between decision-making styles of 
executives and their demographics in Qatar. 
1.7 THEORETICAL MODEL 
Krabuanrat (1997) investigated Strategic Decision Making for Thai Executive Managers, 
concluding that in all cases the strategic decisions involved the use of both rational and intuitive 
elements in combination and that rationality vs heuristic could be regarded as opposite ends of one 
dimension; instead, both elements appear to contribute to practical decisions. Furthermore, the 
higher the strategic level of the problem, the higher the rational content of the decision-making 
process becomes. In Figure 1.1 below, Krabuanrat (1997) described the following types of decision 
making within organisations. 
Figure 1.1 
Types of Decision-Making (Krabuanrat, 1997)   
There is a group decision making process, which involves more than one person to 
conclude the final decision and the individual decision-making process in which the decision 
maker has full authority to make the decision alone. Within the Group decision making process, 
there are the Garbage Can Model and Power and Politics Model. The Garbage Can process is one 
in which problems, solutions and participants move from one choice of opportunity to another in 
such a way that the nature of the choice, the time it takes and the problems it solves all depend on 
a relatively complicated intermeshing of elements. The Powers and Politics model suggests that 
decision-makers with conflicting preference engage in political debate to identify a favorable 
decision. 
The individual decision-making process includes the Heuristics model, which is based on 
intuition or educated guesswork, and Quasi-rational model. Accordingly, this research intends to 
answer the following questions regarding the heuristics model: 
 Are they used in the strategic decision-making process? 
 How much are they used? 
 Are they used together with the rationality model? 
 Are they used with high levels of strategic decision-making? 
A conspicuous gap lies between the rational-choice paradigm and the behavioural 
decision-making paradigm. The missing model is heuristic (research-based) yet rational (non-
biasing): a set of methods for the logically sound discovery and design of economic actions, 
options, and objectives. Such a model is developed in this paper, enriching the notion and repertory 
of heuristics that are typically considered in economic and cognitive sciences with the notion and 
set of heuristics typically considered in the philosophy of science and the logic of discovery. The 
model then provides a response to the hitherto unaddressed question of how knowledge, on which 
the rationality and wisdom of any decision largely depends, can be constructed in a valid and 
reliable way. The specification of the heuristics that can effectively and efficiently guide economic 
discovery and innovation seems to be overdue in an economy said to be knowledge intensive and 
innovation oriented. This study investigates all possible decision-making styles found in the 
literature (Scott and Bruce, 1995), (Dew et al., 2009), (Ballou,1989), (Williams, 2010) and the 
study operationalises them as independent variables to analyse their effect on decision making 
process as reflected by respondents from executive decision makers in Qatar. The reason for that 
is to find out what other alternatives in terms of decision-making styles other than rational and 
heuristic, like intuitive, dependent, avoidant, and spontaneous decision-making styles founded in 
the literature that executive decision makers in Qatar might pursue, accordingly, this study propose 
the following theoretical model as shown in Figure 1.2. 
Figure 1.2 
Theoretical Model  
Rational Decision-making Style 
Intuitive Decision-making Style 
Dependent Decision-making Style 
Avoidant Decision-making Style 
Spontaneous Decision-making Style 
Expert Heuristic Decision-making Style 
Decision-making Styles  
Decision-making Process 
1.8 RESEARCH METHODOLOGY  
This study uses a Mixed Methods approach. However, qualitative type of research is 
generally discouraged by natural scientists, but it is of great importance for social scientists 
(Creswell, 2014). It is usually criticised due to its inability to be generalized, but it is more reality 
based and more flexible in nature. A qualitative analysis was conducted for this research by 
performing face to face interviews. The purpose of selecting face to face interviews for this 
qualitative analysis was to collect as much rich data as possible and to construct a strong 
foundation from which to establish the body of results and conclusions. This method allowed 
insight into not just words but body language, gestures and facial expressions allowing a good 
balance of flexibility and consistency. In this case, the researcher collected quantitative data using 
an on-line Monkey Survey questionnaire completed by 350 Qatari Executive Managers of all 
levels of management both public sector and private sector of various business areas (Appendix 
1). Qualitative data was first collected from semi-structured interviews with four top Qatari senior 
managers (Appendix 2) to provide a preview of decision-making contexts in Qatar. 
1.9 IMPORTANCE OF THE STUDY 
Since the the blockade on Qatar, which was started in June 2017, pressure has increased 
on Senior Managers in Qatar to maintain standards, staff morale and profitability in an increasingly 
uncertain geo-political and economic environment. Therefore, the frequency and importance of 
executive decisions has increased dramatically and so have the costs of making wrong strategic 
decisions. Therefore, this study comes at a critical period for many senior managers in Qatar, who 
should use rational and heuristic elements to facilitate the executive decision-making process.  
1.10  POTENTIAL CONTRIBUTIONS OF THE STUDY  
The utility of this study and its contribution on the individual-difference decision-making 
paradigm within academic research is related to the possibility of developing a wider and more 
comprehensive taxonomy of rational and heuristic decision-making process, still grounded on an 
evidence-based approach. Adopting evidence- based categories of rational and heuristic may allow 
future research to investigate more precisely the presence of multiple processes in decision-making 
against the idea of a single decision-making competence (Ceschi et al., 2017). On the other hand, 
even if the present study showed the presence of consistent relationships between some traits and 
rational and heuristic, some of the variance remains unexplained by leaving room to other possible 
predictors (expertise, tacit knowledge, and self-regulation mood) Which have proved to have a 
role in the Anchoring and Adjustment). Such variables, in comparison to individual differences, 
may be developable characteristics, which would be possible to train to avoid certain rational or 
heuristic decision-making styles.  Understanding strategies by which individuals approach 
decisions and improve them has relevant implications for physical, mental, and financial well-
being. It is important to recall how several studies have shown that the tendency to respond 
irrationally is associated with problematic behaviours and outcomes across the lifespan 
(Dieckmann, & Weller, 2011; Weller et al., 2012). This study designed to promote better decision-
making process within Qatari executives in both public and private sectors through by 
recommending debiasing strategies that might consider present evidence-based dimensions of 
rational and heuristic decision-making styles that might have better impact on real-world decision 
outcomes. 
1.11 LITERATURE GAPS AND UNIQUENESS OF THE STUDY 
The uniqueness of this research stems from two factors. The first is using self-reporting 
approach (questionnaire), to explore the culture and the awareness of the managers in Qatar about 
their own practice. The second is, identifying the dominant culture within the managers 
community by revealing the discrepancies in self-reported practice. By these two elements, this 
study got the opportunity to propose the direction of improvement and its general requirement.  
While research studying Qatari economy is tremendous and covers many perspectives; exploring 
management as a driving force to understand possibilities of transformation, is not found.  By 
focusing on analyzing self-reporting attitude within managers in private and public sectors, this 
research attempts to explore the direction of changing decision-making model within the Qataris 
economy, as a driving force for economic transformation. Five gaps in the literature are identified: 
1) The degree to which heuristic decision-making style are used in executive decision-making 
process in Qatar. 2) The degree to which rational decision-making style are used in executive 
decision-making process in Qatar. 3) The relation between rational and heuristics in executive 
decision-making process. 4) The best practices of rational and heuristic elements during executive 
decision-making process. 5) The relation of both elements (heuristics and rational) with the 
Executive Decision-making process 
1.12 THE STRUCTURE OF THIS THESIS  
This thesis is organised in six chapters as follows.  
Chapter One provides an introduction and background of this research. It explores the underlying 
motivations for researching decision-making styles. This chapter also discusses the research 
context by providing a concise theoretical background of the research related domains. Further, 
the chapter highlights the importance of the research and explains the research objectives and its 
approach in fulfilling the defined objectives. 
Chapter Two presents a comprehensive and critical review of the literature related to decision-
making styles. by doing this critical evaluation of these works in relation to the research problem 
being investigated, this will place each work in the context of its contribution to understand the 
research problem being studied and reveal any gaps that exist in the literature, and more 
importantly, to locate this research within the context of existing literature. 
Chapter Three reviews research methodology including criteria for judging research design and 
the associated research methods, population and sample, data collection, and data analyses. A 
quantitative research approach using survey questionnaire as well as different data analysis 
techniques as appropriate to address research questions. 
Chapter Four presents two major topics: analyses of the survey data collected from Qatari 
different levels of management, and results from addressing five reduced components using factor 
analysis techniques.  
Chapter Five presents discussions. This study presents the development and preliminary 
validation of an instrument to assess decision making styles within executive decision makers in 
Qatar, accordingly this chapter reports the research results and conclusion. Also, the chapter 
highlights the research contribution both to theory and practice. Thereafter, the chapter provides 
recommendations concerning the methodology and methods that are consistent with the research 
results. Finally, the chapter provides important avenues for future research in this area. 
Chapter Six presents the conclusion, which will wrap up the main ideas and summarise the overall 
arguments and findings. 
CHAPTER TWO:  LITERATURE REVIEW 
2.1 INTRODUCTION  
In todays world, organisations are faced with thousands of decisions daily, and how they 
make these decisions will have a huge impact on their financial status (Forrester, 2003). These 
decisions set the tone for the entire organisation in terms of image, profits, and customer service. 
That is why, it is especially important that organisations adopt best practices and execute good 
judgment when it comes to making decisions, because the right decision at the right time could 
help companies achieve great success whereas a wrong decision could end up costing them dearly 
(Capgemini, 2004). 
Businesses are nowadays faced with complex and ill-defined decision problems. Decision 
making is an organisational process of paramount importance. Yet, evidence in organisational 
decision making suggests that businesses incur substantial losses due to bad practice in decision 
making (Papamichail & Rajarm, 2007). 
 Top management teams make strategic decisions, the quality of which influences 
organisational performance. Because consensus among team members facilitates the 
implementation of those decisions, consensus also influences organisational performance. Further, 
to sustain their ability to produce and implement strategic decisions, top management teams must 
maintain positive affective relationships among their members. Thus, decision quality, consensus, 
and affective acceptance are, together, all necessary for sustainable high performance (Amason, 
1996). Strategic decisions address complex and ambiguous issues that involve large amounts of 
organisational resources (Mason & Mitroff, 1981). Thus, it is not surprising that strategic decision 
making has been so heavily re- searched. One stream of this research, focusing on the quality of 
the decisions themselves, has identified two principal antecedents of decision quality: the cognitive 
capabilities of a top management team and the interaction process through which the team 
produces its decisions (Amason, 1996). 
Nowadays the role of senior executives includes a large amount of decision-making and 
complex problem solving. The ability of organisations to take efficient and effective decisions can 
have a major impact on business sustainability and agility (Forrester, 2003). Organisations realize 
the significant positive impact of developing true decision competencies and only realize this when 
solid, high-quality decision-making becomes embedded in the fabric of their DNA (Spetzler, 
2007). 
The most important job of any manager is making decisions. It is also the hardest and the 
most dangerous. With decisions valued in their millions (Forrester, 2003) a bad decision can 
damage a business and a career irreparable (Hammond et al., 2006). Fragmented and inaccurate 
data causes executives and managers to make delayed and flawed decisions costing millions 
(Forrester, 2003). Finding the right data at the right time and analyzing it fast enough remains a 
challenge for businesses as poor decisions can be very costly (Teradata, 2006). 
Managers within organisations are often making bad decisions, solving the wrong 
problems, and ignoring uncertainty (Forrester, 2003). According to (Forrester, 2003), managers 
make bad decisions and find it hard to decide because of decision biases, they do not want to give 
anything up nor do they want to make mistakes. That is why they procrastinate and only make 
decisions when events force them to. Managers often solve the wrong problems because they lack 
a structure for making decisions. As a result, they do not generate value-creating alternatives, they 
look for quick and partial solutions and they fail to seek out all the necessary data or clarify 
objectives (Forrester, 2003). Managers also tend to ignore uncertainty they focus on a single 
outcome, such as the most likely case, and take refuge in ambiguity or imprecise language. 
Managers use the complexity of uncertainty as an excuse for not deciding (Forrester, 2003). 
According to Steward (2004), a good review should be: Comprehensive where evidence 
should be gathered from all relevant sources,  fully referenced; allowing others to follow the path 
of the author to the papers conclusion,  selective; using appropriate search strategies to find the 
key evidence, relevant; focusing on pertinent data, a synthesis of key themes and ideas, balanced; 
between different ideas and opinions, critical; in its appraisal of the literature, and analytical; 
developing new ideas and understandings from the evidence. 
To achieve an understanding of executive decision-making process and how it may be 
improved by integrating the rational and heuristic elements, a literature review has been conducted 
by going over relevant papers, journals, articles, books, conferences and dissertations. The review 
of the literature was conducted by a selection of several authors on the executive decision-making 
field in the last decades. The executive decision-making topic is an overly broad area with 
implications not just in management, but also in many other subjects such as psychology, 
sociology, mathematics, and economics. Starting from the broad concept of Decision-making, this 
research narrows down the subject to make sense of a specific topic, which is the executive 
decision-making process in executives and the role that different decision-making styles play in it.  
This research examined executive decision-making in the business world from a time frame 
perspective. Looking at the evolution of this concept resulted in further understanding the 
directions this phenomenon took during the past decades and the current state of the research. 
Gaining an insight into what has been done until now is crucial to comprehend the possible future 
outcomes and direction of the research. The collection of relevant literature was made mainly 
through revising several scientific journals about management, executives, and organisations. The 
time frame for this research considered the 1950s until the most recent years. The main core of 
the literature is based on previous studies conducted mainly in the last two decades. This provided 
a support for the conceptual analysis which is based on the most modern and updated studies on 
the executive decision-making field.  
2.2 EXECUTIVE DECISION-MAKING  
Mintzberg (1973) defines decision-making as a commitment to action. It can also be 
considered as the process of making intentional, informed choices (Marakas, 2003) between 
alternative actions that are possible. Paradice & Dejoie (1991) defines decision-making as the 
product of intellectual processes resulting in the selection of a course of action between numerous 
options, leading to a final selection. Decision-making is an essential part of management, and it 
occurs in every function at all levels, often taken in the face of uncertainty, conflicting objectives, 
value preference, and risk attitudes. 
The most important job of any manager is making decisions. It is also the hardest and the 
most dangerous. With decisions valued in their millions (Forrester, 2003) a bad decision can 
damage a business and a career irreparable (Hammond et al., 2006). Fragmented and inaccurate 
data causes executives and managers to make delayed and flawed decisions costing millions. 
Finding the right data at the right time and analyzing it fast enough remains a challenge for 
businesses as poor decisions can be very costly (Teradata, 2006). 
Nowadays, decision makers are expected to make more decisions than ever before, often 
without much support and with a limited amount of time. As the complexity of decision-making 
increases, the issue of developing decision-making capabilities remains a challenge (Papamichail 
& Rajarm, 2007; Francis, 2016). 
Literature provides a broad range of definitions concerning Decision-making. The research 
can focus either on the decision itself or the process that leads to a decision (Harrison, 1996). 
Simon (1960) for instance, states I shall be referring not merely to the final act of choice among 
alternatives, but rather to the whole process of decision (p.1). Harrison instead defined the concept 
of decision as a moment in an ongoing process of evaluating alternatives for meeting an 
objective (1996, p.46). In addition to this, Ofstad (1961) described management decision as a 
judgment regarding what one ought to do in a certain situation after having deliberated on some 
alternative courses of actions (1961, p.5). Mintzberg et al. (1976) described a decision as a 
specific commitment to action (usually a commitment of resources) and a decision process as a 
set of actions and dynamic factors that begins with the identification of a stimulus for action and 
ends with the specific commitment to action (p. 246). 
An executive decision can be defined in many ways. Mador (2000) notes that these kinds 
of decisions are characterized by complexity and a high level of uncertainty. Eisenhardt and 
Zbaracki (1992) describe them as infrequent decisions made by the top leaders of an organisation 
that critically affect organisational health and survival (p. 17). On the same line is Harrison 
(1996), for whom executive decisions are those made by top managers and able to set the tone 
and tempo of managerial decision-making for every individual and unit throughout the entire 
organization (p. 46). Hickson et al. (1986) add that a decision may be considered executive in one 
industry, but the same decision may very well be considered less executive or not executive in a 
different one. For instance, an executive decision could be the launch of a new product such as a 
new car in the automotive industry. However, the launch of a new product such as a new toy in a 
factory that produces hundreds of toys every year might not be executive (Elbanna, 2006). 
Regarding the characteristics of an executive decision, Elbanna (2006) distinguishes four 
different attributes: the association with high risks and uncertainties, the interrelations with other 
decisions in the organisation, the fact that they do not have one best solution among the others, 
and it is hard to reverse the decision once it has been made. Shirley (1982) instead, provides a set 
of five distinctive criteria to identify these decisions: (1) the decision needs to define the 
relationship between the organisation and its environment, (2) the decision must be affecting the 
whole organisation, (3) the decision has to be based on inputs from different organisational areas, 
(4) the decision must provide constraints and directions for all the administrative and operational 
activities of the organisation, (5) the decision needs to be salient for the long term success of the 
organisation. (Mador 2000), after an extensive literature review and empirical studies on decision-
making, identified a few common characteristics to recognize good executive decisions: 
comprehensiveness, extensiveness, and speed (2000, p.217). 
From the previous considerations it is possible to understand the importance that such 
decisions have for an organisation. They give origin to several other decisions of lesser magnitude 
through all the descending management levels of the company (Harrison, 1996; Francis, 2016; 
Ogarca, 2015). On this point, Harrison (1996) specifies that a wrong decision taken at the top of 
the organisation is likely to have the same negative effect on the lower levels of management. 
Therefore, executive decisions are responsible for the future outcomes of the organisation seen in 
its entirety and, most of all, for its survival (Francis, 2016; Ogarca, 2015). 
2.3 EXECUTIVE DECISION-MAKING PROCESS 
Executive decision-making process is growing of importance because of the characteristics of 
strategic decisions (Papula, & Papulova, 2015): they are large-scale, risky and hard to reverse; they 
are a bridge between deliberate and emerging strategies; they can be a major source of 
organisational learning; they play an important part in the development of individual managers, 
and they cut across functions and academic disciplines. Joshi et al. (2018) indicated that the nature 
of the decision to be made will influence the nature of the process to be used. 
Executive decision-making process is crucial because it involves fundamental decisions 
which shape the course of a firm, thus the approach to Executive decision-making process should 
be considered very carefully. First, a brief look at some views and approach to decision-making. 
The current environment is in constant motion, and it is necessary to respond to ongoing changes. 
This knowledge is not new. As already ancient philosopher Heraclitus pointed out "Panta Rhei" - 
everything flows. The change and life are interdependent and interconnected. People can adapt to 
living in changing environment due to their thinking ability. It is known how to monitor 
environment, how to identify the effects, how to understand and how to make decisions.  
The process of human thought reflected the well-known philosopher Immanuel Kant 
(Papula, & Papulova, 2015) when he stated that all knowledge begins with the perception, then 
proceeds to the understanding and ends with judgment/idea, on this basis, a model of thinking and 
decision-making can be proposed to describe how people are reaching decisions or how the people 
should approach decision-making as shown in Figure 2.1. 
Figure 2.1 
The process of thinking and decision-making 
Decision-making is undoubtedly one of the basic management functions despite the fact 
that the founders of management theory set the basic functions of management as planning, 
organising, leading people and controlling (Robbins & Coulter, 2013). Decision-making has an 
important cross-cutting feature that is organically incorporated into all other management 
functions. The importance of decision-making and the need for a separate investigation and 
improvement has begun to draw attention in the sixties of the twentieth century by Simon (1960), 
who pointed out the need to understand the decision-making as a process as shown in Figure 2.2. 
Figure 2.2  
Decision-making process by Simon (1960) 
The decision-making theory, which is based on the initiative of Simon (1960), has begun 
to develop since then. Since that time, theory has created a multitude of analytical methods and 
techniques that are used in detecting opportunities, searching, and developing of alternative 
solutions, as well as making choice of suitable alternatives to solve problems. Various techniques 
were created also for decision-making at the executive level. For example, AlHussayen (2009) 
presents twenty-three rules and guidelines for improving organisational decision making, 
including: understanding the environment, having top management support, using available 
resources, embracing uncertainty, considering the long and short term, clearly stating goals and 
objectives, problem identification, objectivity, creating alternatives, setting clear criteria and 
sticking to them, using reliable information, being open to information, using experience, 
consulting, using gut feeling, having distinct roles and responsibilities, having shared values and 
beliefs, having good communication, having the appropriate group composition, having a devils 
advocate, allocation of time, realistic assessment of execution capabilities and commitment to 
follow-through. Importantly, AlHussayen (2009) also presents a conceptual framework (ePIPE) 
for improving organisational decision making: that considers the environment, the purpose, the 
information, the people and the execution when making decisions. 
According to Harrison (1996) decision-making is the principal activity that differentiates 
the role of the manager from other occupations in the society. On the same line is Drucker (1967) 
that states to make the important decision is the specific executive task (p. 98). The significance 
of decision-making for an organisation is highlighted by Cornell (1980), who states that of all the 
managerial functions that executives perform, the act of making a decision is without equal in 
importance (p. 13). Similarly, McLaughlin (1995) claims that organisations reach success when 
they outperform their competitors in at least three different ways: by making better decisions, by 
deciding faster and by implementing more decisions. 
The studies on decision-making processes started to take place in the business world when 
Chester Barnard placed it at the center of the executives functions (Barnard, 1938). Since then, 
the research on decision-making went through different approaches. For many years in the last 
century, decisions have been seen and studied as phenomena driven exclusively by a rational mind 
(Langley et al., 1995), even though bounded (Simon, 1979). According to Langley et al. (1995) 
this approach to the study of decision-making has produced a credible description of the 
phenomenon, but it has failed in explaining its most significant aspects. To give a better 
explanation about strategic Decision-making, the research started to consider other elements in 
relation to rationality, such as intuitive processes and political models (Khatri & Ng, 2000; Langley 
et al., 1995; Eisenhardt & Zbaracki, 1992; Francis., 2016; Ogarca, 2015). Therefore, rationality 
and intuition are now accepted as both valuable for strategic decision-making (Calabretta et al., 
2017). 
The literature in decision-making processes divides the decision-making models in 
structured, and unstructured or less structured models. When they are structured, authors talk about 
a rational approach, where each phase can be distinguished and individually defined, to help 
decision makers to make better decisions (Francis, 2016; Ogarca, 2015). Unstructured or less 
structured models are, instead, the ones considering other elements, such as a political behaviour 
view, according to which the strategic decision is formed in a process in which decision makers, 
having different interests, try to influence others opinion to achieve their goals (Elbanna, 2006). 
Another unstructured approach sees the strategic decision as the outcome of an intuitive process, 
focused more on the emotions and the senses of the managers, rather than structured models 
(Gigerenzer, 2001; Simon, 1993; Francis, 2016; Ogarca, 2015). 
An example of a structured model is described in six main functions as follows: (1) setting 
managerial objectives, (2) searching for alternatives, (3) comparing and evaluating alternatives, 
(4) the act of choice, (5) implementing the decision, and (6) follow-up and control (Harrison,1996; 
Song et al., 2017; Zhang et al., 2019). The process starts with setting objectives and scanning the 
internal and external environment of the organisation helps to gather information to set different 
alternatives. At this point the alternatives are compared and evaluated, and only after this point the 
decision maker makes his choice. The decision now needs to be transformed in an operational 
reality by implementing it. The last phase concerns the coincidence of the initial objective and the 
actual outcome (Harrison, 1996). 
Another model is described by Corner et al. (1994). The authors in this case studied the 
interaction between the organisational and individual levels in the strategic decision-making 
process. The first stage is the attention. The top managers focus their attention on a specific 
information about a strategic decision before processing it. The second stage is the encoding, 
which means to interpret the information. This phase is usually characterized by biased decisions 
because of the inclusion of previous existing knowledge. The third stage is the storage or 
retrieval, which means the preservation of the information previously interpreted. In this case 
the link between the individual and the organisational level is the socialization. This can be seen 
when senior top executives influence newcomers with routines and already accepted knowledge. 
The next stage is the decision. Mintzberg et al. (1976) agreed that decisions on a strategical level 
came from the information that was previously stored. After the decision comes the stage of 
action. This is the enactment of the strategic decision. On the individual level this is the action 
of the executive within the personal sphere of responsibility. On the organisational level it is the 
coordinated action of the different people involved in the process to implement a strategic decision. 
Finally, the outcomes are the results of the implementation of the decision, and can be either on 
an individual level, as for example in terms of personal evaluation, or on the organisational level 
in terms of financial returns (Corner et al., 1994). 
2.4 RATIONAL ELEMENTS  
2.4.1 RATIONALITY 
Rationality in decision-making is part of the broader discussion about economic rationality 
(Langley et al., 1995; Francis, 2016). According to Elbanna (2006), the level of rationality in 
strategic decision-making has played an important role as one of its main elements, and it has been 
intensively investigated by many researchers. Rationality as the relationship between means that 
humans use to reach certain ends, ends which themselves become means to reach other ends. 
Weber: distinguishes between substantive and formal rationality; substantive rationality designates 
material behaviour shaped by political, religious or ethical standards; formal rationality refers to 
action based on calculation and means-to-ends reasoning (Fforde, 2018). 
There are several definitions of rationality depending on the specific field in which the 
research puts its focus. In the economic perspective, rationality often refers to the maximization of 
utility; in this view the assumption is that individuals tend to maximize their expected utility 
(Elbanna & Child, 2007). The theory of expected utility was formulated for the first time by 
Neumann & Morgenstern (1953 cited in Fox, 2015). Right after its formulation other authors began 
to apply this model of rational behaviour as the explanation of how people make decisions (Fox, 
2015).  
Simon (1978) was one of the first researchers to challenge this notion in the 1950s. According to 
Simon (1978 cited in Langley, et, al., 1995), decision makers are not omniscient, and their 
rationality is limited by their own capabilities. Simon (1978)s view remained confined, however, 
in what was lately defined as the cerebral rationality (Langley et al.,1995, p.262). Simon (1978) 
kept seeing the decision-making as a cognitive process that could be easily explained by a step-to-
step succession (Langley et al.,1995). On the same line as Simon (1978), Butler (2002) gives a 
definition indicating that Rationality is the reason for doing something and to judge a behaviour 
as reasonable is to be able to say that the behaviour is understandable within a given frame of 
reference (p. 226). Alternatively, Dean & Sharfman (1993) claim rationality represents the type 
of behaviour that is logical in pursuing goals. Eisenhardt & Zbaracki (1992) state that rationality 
follows the assumption that human behaviour has some purpose (p. 18) and this usually is 
expressed in a model of rational action. Put another way, rationality characterizes that behaviour, 
which is logical in pursuing goals, this broad conception underlies many social science models of 
rationality. Rational choice is championed by a wide range of disciplines, because of its ability as 
a theory to tie together so many of the social sciences within one rubric (Lazo et al., 2015). 
Given the historical evolution of rationality, scholars developed some constructs of 
rationality to be distinguished from more global conceptions of rationality, which have overtones 
of decision-maker omniscience (Simon, 1978). These constructs represent measures of the extent 
to which the SDMP approximates the rational model of decision-making (see Table 2.1). In this 
case, decision-makers are rational to the limits of their own capabilities (bounded rationality); as 
Snyman & Drew (2003) stress, bounded rationality emphasises the decision-making process which 
is limited by cognitive and political realities. Given these limitations, decision-makers aspire to 
achieve objectives which are good enough rather than the best (Eisenhardt, 1997) as shown in 
Table 2.1 (below). 
Although those constructs all derive from the rational model of decision-making, there are 
many differences between them, and various authors have used different labels to define 
rationality. 
Moreover, studies which use the same label may use different indicators to operationalize 
it. For example, Khatri (1994) uses comprehensiveness as one indicator of what he calls strategic 
rationality. He measures this indicator, using one item of a Likert-type scale, while both 
Fredrickson & Mitchell (1984) and Jones et al. (1992) divide comprehensiveness into two 
components: analytical and integrative. Although both Fredrickson & Mitchell (1984) and Jones 
et al. (1992) divide comprehensiveness into the same two components and define them in a similar 
way, they operationalize them differently. 
The theory of bounded rationality was proposed for the first time in the 1950s as an 
alternative to the classical concept of rationality (Langley et al., 1995). Snyman & Drew (2003) 
point out that bounded rationality centers on the decision-making process which is limited by 
cognitive and political realities. Because of these restrictions, decision makers attempt to reach 
goals which are sufficient rather than optimal (Eisenhardt, 1997). Claims from Eisenhardt & 
Zbaracki (1992) support that empirical research has proven decision makers are more boundedly 
rational than purely rational. They refer to the cognitive limits of the rational decision-making 
model, the complexity of problems and the conflicts between decision makers which form the 
decision process. 
There are three main obstacles in adopting a rational decision-making process, as identified 
by Jones et al. (1992). First, the organisation may lack the necessary recourses to find and examine 
the respective information, secondly, the cognitive abilities of decision makers could be limited 
and, thirdly, managers could be reluctant to stir up the political structure of the organisation (Jones 
et al.,1992). 
Rational processes have been a central part of strategic decision-making and a great amount 
of theoretical and empirical research has been conducted on the topic (Elbanna & Child, 2007). 
The interplay between rational decision processes and organisational outcomes has divided the 
scholars in the field and no consensus has been reached (Goll & Rasheed, 1997). Elbanna (2006) 
mentioned that there is empirical evidence for all kinds of possible relationships between the 
rationality and organisational outcomes, positive relationship, negative relationship, or no 
relationship whatsoever. For instance, Fredrickson & Mitchell (1984) identified a negative 
relationship between rationality and performance in an unstable environment, whereas a positive 
relationship was proven in a stable environment (Fredrickson, 1984). In contrast to the findings of 
Fredrickson and his colleagues, Dean & Sharfman (1996) found a stronger relationship between 
rationality and decision effectiveness in unstable environments, rather than in stable ones. 
Furthermore, Eisenhardt & Bourgeois (1988) concluded that a positive relationship between 
rationality and firm performance exists in a high-velocity environment.
