With regards to the research question, Yin (1994) argues the following: If research 
questions focus mainly on what questions, there are two possibilities. The questions can focus 
on exploratory measures, or they can focus on identifying outcomes. This research focuses on 
exploratory measures. A question like this is a reasonable sign for conducting an exploratory study, 
with the goal to develop pertinent hypotheses and propositions for further inquiry (Yin, 1994). As 
an exploratory study, any of the five research strategies can be used. Yin (1994) adds to that, that 
it is possible to use more than one strategy in any given study (for example, a questionnaire within 
a case study or a case study within a survey).  
To summarize, the most important condition for differentiating among the various research 
strategies, is to identify the research question that is being asked. The second important 
determinant of the strategy is the extent of control that is needed over behavioural events. In the 
case of this exploratory research, no control over behavioural events is needed. Regarding the third 
point, the degree of focus on contemporary as supposed to historical events, it is clear that this 
research focuses on contemporary events. So, if looking at Table 3.2 of Yin (1994) this research 
concludes that a questionnaire within the multiple case study is the most appropriate design. In the 
event due largely to the restrictions faced by the COVID 19 pandemic that occurred at the 
beginning of the qualitative data collection phase and ensuing difficulties in obtaining interviews 
only three interviews with individual executives were possible and the research was redirected to 
focus of the quantitative design. 
3.4.1 QUANTITATIVE RESEARCH DESIGN 
To create a valid and reliable questionnaire it is important to create the right procedures. In 
this way conducting the questionnaire can have a major effect on the likelihood that the resulting 
data will describe accurately what they intended to describe (Fowler, 2009). A questionnaire is 
used because it is a cost-efficient, practical, fast, scalable, anonymous, confidential and it will 
cover all the aspects of the topics (Drnyei & Taguchi, 2009). When a questionnaire is used as a 
data collection method, three different methodologies must be used. First, the sampling method, 
then the questions must be designed and after that the data must be collected (Fowler, 2009). The 
following points should be considered during research design: 
1) Feasibility of the research project. 
For the project to be useful within the scope of the research (in terms of timings, budget 
and access to organisations) it is important to be specific about the companies that are suited for 
this research. To be able to complete the research in time and within the scope of this thesis, it is 
important to target at medium-sized companies or independent divisions of larger companies.  
2) The principle of theoretical sampling  
The selection of the cases must be in line with the principles of theoretical sampling 
according to Eisenhardt (1989). The goal of theoretical sampling is to choose cases which are 
likely to replicate or extend the emergent theory (Eisenhardt, 1989, p. 537). So, it is sampling 
based on non-statistical reasons.  
3) Transparency of the process  
To create results, the company should be aware of the problem that information overload 
can create at multiple levels in the company. The company should also be interested in creating 
solutions regarding this problem they face.  
4) Access to multiple hierarchical levels  
The strategic decision-making takes place at an individual level but has an effect on both the 
individual level and the organisational level. That is why this research is focused on decision-
making in the context of organisations to be able to get a better overview of the problems that arise 
and the solutions that may result from the collected data. So, to have control on both the individual 
and organisational level, access to multiple hierarchical levels is required.  
5) The information-intensive type of work  
The participants of the questionnaire must be connected to the strategic decision-making 
process of the organisation and must be aware what heuristics and biases are. Therefore, the focus 
is on strategic decision-makers within organisations. 
3.4.2 QUALITATIVE RESEARCH DESIGN 
The current study implements a qualitative research design. This technique is employed to 
describe any data collection or data analysis that generates or uses non-numerical data (Saunders 
et al., 2012). In management research, the use of language data provides the opportunity to gain 
insights into social and organisational realities (Easterby-Smith et al., 2012, p. 129). The 
exploratory study approach was exercised, which is suitable when uncertainty about the precise 
nature of a problem is identified (Saunders et al., 2012).  
The research strategy applied is the qualitative case study, which facilitates exploration 
of a phenomenon within its context using a variety of data sources (Baxter & Jack, 2008, p. 544). 
According to Yin (2003), there are four distinctive conditions which favor the use of a case study:  
 When the study focuses on answering how and why questions, 
 When the researcher cannot manipulate the behaviour of the people involved in the study,  
 When the researcher needs to investigate contextual conditions, relevant to the phenomenon 
under study, and 
 When the boundaries between the phenomenon and the context are ambiguous.  
Since this research will contain more than one single case, the selected method is the 
multiple-case study. The logic behind the use of multiple cases is based on whether results can be 
replicated among cases (Saunders et al., 2012). Baxter & Jack (2008) claim that the multiple-case 
study is used when the researcher examines several cases to understand the similarities and 
differences among the cases. The evidence produced by a multiple-case study is reliable and 
robust, but on the other hand it can also be very time consuming and costly (Baxter & Jack, 2008). 
The time constraints for conducting this research and the collection of data over a short period of 
time imposed the choice of a cross-sectional study, serving as a snapshot of a particular 
phenomenon at a particular time (Saunders et al., 2012). 
3.5  DATA COLLECTION METHOD 
To investigate the theoretical model, a custom designed questionnaire was administered 
and a series of semi-structured interviews, meaning that they were based on a set of questions that 
were prepared beforehand, but the interviewees were encouraged to reflect on their personal 
experiences, so that better insights could be gained. The choice of discursive interviews was made 
in line with the qualitative data method and the initial subjective research approach. The interview 
questions were formulated considering the three main aspects of analysis; the successful decision-
making case dimensions, the rational elements, and the heuristic elements, as presented in the 
theoretical model, in Chapter 2.  
The interviews were conducted directly in the main offices of the companies. All the 
interviews were recorded, and their duration was between 40 and 48 minutes. The interview 
questions, collected relevant information, have been elaborated by the researcher and comprised 
of 21 questions. This basic set of questions provided us with general, as well as complex 
information about the participants. The interview questions were also written considering the role 
and professional background of the participants. Given that all of them were executives, holding a 
top management position, it was decided to use the same basic set of questions for all of them. 
According to each specific executive more specific questions could be integrated in to the 
interview.  
The interview questions (see Appendix 2) were structured in different parts. The first part, 
or the introduction part, gained general information about the position and the role of the 
participant, the organisatinal structure of the company, the performance of the company, and 
relevant facts about the internal and the external environment in which the organisation operates. 
The relevance of gaining this information can be found in the fact that they provide a background 
of the executives and the environment in which they operate. Furthermore, the relation between 
these characteristics and the executive decision-making process, as well as the role of rational and 
heuristic could be extremely relevant for the aim of the research. The second part of the interview 
questions concerned the introduction to the heart of the study, the executive decision-making 
process. The participants were asked to make an example of a successful executive decision-
making in which they were involved in the past. By focusing the attention on a specific example 
provided a more reliable set of data, avoiding abstract conceptualization regarding general 
executive decisions. In this second section, there are two subgroups of question. The first subgroup 
reveals understanding of the extension of the rationality approach in that situation. The second 
subgroup explored the role of heuristic and the implication that personal judgments and 
professional experience had in that specific case. By submitting this set of questions to the 
participant the goal was to gather reliable qualitative data to support the conceptual analysis.  
The present multiple-case study examined each executive as a different case, focusing on 
their individual executive decision-making processes. Therefore, the spotlight was turned on each 
manager, each within different organisational environments. A total of four executives belonging 
to three different companies participated in the research and the interviews were conducted in 
Qatar. 
The data will be collected with the use of questionnaires that are operationalized in a way 
that the constructs (rational and heuristic) can be measured in a viable and reliable way. The 
empirical data will be collected in the period from November 2019 to January 2020. The 
questionnaires will be designed by the author and will be collected online. 
The main instrument for data collection in this study is the use of questionnaires and 
interview. It depends on the organisations where the case study will be conducted, but the sample 
size will be around four questionnaires at three companies, so twelve in total. The number of 
questionnaires for this multiple case study is determined in consultation with the supervisor. The 
goal of these questionnaires is to determine if the interviewees used heuristics and rational while 
enduring information overload in the context of executive decision-making process and what effect 
it had on them and the organisation. As stated in the second chapter, information overload is 
studied from an organisational perspective (Bazerman & Moore, 2008). To determine the amount 
of information overload that the respondents face, general questions about information overload 
in an organisational environment will be asked.  
The representativeness rational and heuristic will be measured in the following way. Fong 
& Nisbett (1991) and Fong et al. (1986) use an approach to measure these rational and heuristic. 
They developed scenarios which represented real-life executive decisions, which led to two 
alternatives. The first alternative is based on quantitative or statistical information; the other 
alternative is based on rational and heuristic reasoning. Subjects were asked to make the executive-
decision and then describe the reasoning of how they came to their decision. After that one can 
determine whether one of the two reasoning types (rational or heuristic reasoning) was used to 
make the decision. In this research the coding schema that will be used will be closely related to 
the Fong and Nisbett (1991) and the Fong et al. (1986) article to be able to connect all the important 
parts regarding the representativeness rational and heuristic together.  
Executive decision-making process will be measured using the three items developed by 
Amason (1996) to assess the quality of the executive decision made within the cases. In line with 
previous studies, the data will be collected from experts who are involved in the executive 
decision-making process.  
An absolute objective measure of the executive decision-making process of a single 
decision is hard to take in isolation and impossible for the research to assess, therefore a perceptual 
measure of the relative executive decision-making process will be used. This is because a decision 
that is good in one context can create poor results when the context changes. When using an 
objective measure the different decisions imply that each decision has an equal chance of 
producing the preferred outcome. But that is not always true. Because when a team only has the 
option to choose between poor alternatives, an objective measure of executive decision-making 
process would result in the impression that the executive decision-making process of the team was 
worse, than a team that had better alternatives to choose from. However, this does not confirm that 
the first team choose wisely and emerged in a better way than expected, than the other team. 
Therefore, the best way to measure the executive decision-making process is to ask those who 
have observed its effects and observe those who understand its context to judge, retrospectively 
and on several dimensions, how the decision turned out. Therefore, a perceptual measure is used. 
3.6  DATA ANALYSIS 
Since concepts in qualitative research depend on social interpretation, qualitative data have 
the tendency to be more ambiguous, elastic, and complex than quantitative data, so analysis of the 
data ought to be responsive to these attributes to be meaningful (Saunders et al., 2012). The data 
analysis procedure entails a process of summarising data, categorising and grouping them 
according to themes, and finally connecting these categories in such a way that a structure is 
formed to answer the research questions (Saunders et al., 2012). The interviews conducted for the 
purpose of this research were audio-recorded and subsequently transcribed, making use of the 
online transcription and dictation software Transcribe (2017), to perform the transcriptions in a 
time-efficient way. The transcriptions were performed by the authors, so that better insights about 
their personal interviewing style would be gained, whereas the social and emotional perspectives 
of the interviews could be reminded during the transcriptions and an initial analysis of the meaning 
could commence (Brggen &Willems, 2009).  
The analytical process that was employed in the current study was inspired by the 
suggestions of Saunders et al. (2012), which can be summarized in the following steps: identify 
categories or codes to understand the data, develop further analytical categories to pinpoint 
relationships and patterns, develop testable arguments and, finally, draw and verify conclusions. 
In the beginning, the acquired data were divided into 4 categories, based on the structure of the 
interview questions which the participants were called to answer. Building on this analysis and the 
discussion that it initiated, the researchers were able to draw their conclusions on this study. The 
main proposition P1 (Executives in Qatar use a range of decision-making styles, including rational 
and heuristic styles, that can be profiled using a structured questionnaire) and the two main 
hypotheses H01: there is NO Statistically Significant (0.05) difference between decision-making 
styles of executives and their strategic contexts in Qatar and H02: there is NO Statistically 
Significant (0.05) difference between decision-making styles of executives and their 
demographics in Qatar. As described in paragraph 3.1 the heuristic and rational will be measured 
via the Fong & Nisbett (1991) and Fong et al. (1986) approach where real-life scenarios will be 
developed to assess the representativeness in use of the heuristic. The custom designed 
questionnaire will use a 5-point Likert scale, so that data can be analysed to produce a reliable 
factor scale. 
3.7  DATA QUALITY 
According to Saunders et al. (2012) there are data quality issues associated with semi-
structured interviews that need to be overcome in this kind of research. These data quality issues 
are associated with reliability, forms of bias, generalizability, and validity (Saunders et al., 2012).  
The first problem discussed by Saunders et al. (2012) is the concern about reliability. This 
is because semi-structured or in-depth interviews lack of a standardization process. According to 
Saunders et al. (2012) this problem is overcome by the fact that qualitative research assumes that 
the situations to be explored are complex and dynamic. Furthermore, the findings of this kind of 
research approaches do not need to be always repeatable because they come from a specific reality 
and a particular time, that could also change (Saunders et al., 2012). The authors believe that this 
study is a snapshot of the examined situation at a specific, and limited, time frame, though it is 
structured in such a way that could be repeatable in the future by other researchers.  
Connected to reliability is the problem of biases. According to Saunders et al. (2012) there 
are three different kinds of biases that must be considered. The first one is the bias of who conducts 
the interview. This is related to the behavioural approach of the interviewer, that through 
comments or a certain attitude might influence the way the participants answer the questions posed 
(Saunders et al., 2012). Another issue is connected to the interviewee rational that can be called 
also response bias. Saunders et al. (2012) note that this kind of bias may be linked either to the 
perception about the interviewer or to the typical characteristics of the interview process. Saunders 
et al. (2012) argue that the semi-structured interview process, as well as the in-depth one, is 
characterized by an intrusion into the personal sphere of the participant. Even though they might 
be willing to contribute with personal experiences, in some cases they might be sensitive in the 
disclosure of certain information through an unstructured interview process (Saunders et al., 2012). 
The problem in this case is that the participant would give only a partial picture of the situation to 
be explored. The last form of bias analysed by Saunders et al. (2012) is the participation bias. This 
kind of bias is related to the nature of the individuals who agree to participate in the research 
(Saunders et al., 2012). In other words, the interview process takes time, and this factor might 
negatively affect the will of the participants to participate (Saunders et al., 2012).  
According to Saunders et al. (2012) one way to overcome issues of biases is to have a good 
degree of knowledge about the organisation and the culture of the company. Before each interview 
information was gained concerning the companys website and other different sources, such as 
online articles and news. In such a way the researcher became more familiar with the situation at 
hand. Another way to overcome these biases was to provide the least information possible about 
the research to the participants, before conducting the interview. This method allowed us to have 
a more reliable set of data. The interviews were conducted in the main offices of the companies, 
in quiet conference rooms, which enabled the interview process to be more professional and 
therefore the answers to be more accurate. The researcher's behaviour during the interviews was 
also central in trying to overcome these biases. Overall, a neutral behaviour is maintained by the 
researcher to reduce to the minimum extent possible influence on the participants. The interviews 
were conducted to avoid leading questions that would compromise the validity of the data.  
A third problem connected to the semi-structured interview process is the generalizability 
issue. This problem is related to the extension of the research findings that might not be applicable 
to other fields of the research (Saunders et al., 2012). According to Saunders et al. (2012) this 
problem is overcome by the fact that qualitative research is related to the theoretical model chosen 
for the study. This research strived to demonstrate that the findings have a solid meaning from a 
theoretical perspective.  
The last issue that Saunders et al. (2012) consider is the validity problem. This relates to 
the level of extension that the interviewers had in exploring the interviewees knowledge and 
experience, and how they made sense of it (Saunders et al., 2012). This issue was mitigated against 
and the researcher explored the specific situation by making in-depth questions on the subject that 
was relevant for the study. This allowed an extended exploration of the participants knowledge.  
Triangulation, as Long & Johnson (2000) indicate, refers to using various data sources, 
data collection methods, or investigators, to tackle the drawbacks of using any single source, 
method, or investigator. There are several elements related to the design of a case study, so that 
the quality and trustworthiness of the project can be increased. According to DiCenso at al., (2005), 
a researcher is responsible to guarantee that the research questions of the case study are clearly 
defined, the case study design is suitable for the research questions, purposeful sampling strategies 
have been used, data are collected and processed in a systematic way and the data analysis in done 
properly. Triangulation of data sources, types or researchers, Baxter & Jack (2008) claim, is a 
useful strategy to support the principle in case study research, so that each phenomenon is analysed 
from various perspectives. Additional strategies which contribute to the overall credibility include 
the use of reflection or the maintenance of field notes and peer examination of the data (Baxter 
& Jack, 2008, p. 556). In this study, triangulation was achieved through a delegation of interviews 
and tasks. Out of the five interviews, two of them were conducted by a single and different 
researcher, while the other three were conducted by both researchers, but tasks were rotated. More 
specifically, during these interviews, one researcher would commence by posing the questions, 
while the other would keep notes about the most interesting comments of the participant or facial 
expressions and emotional characteristics. After the middle of the interview, roles were exchanged, 
thus minimizing the drawbacks of a single interviewer. 
For the questionnaires, the executive decision-making process will be measured using the 
three items developed by Amason (1996) to assess the quality of the executive decision-making 
process made within the cases. In line with previous studies, the data will be collected from experts 
who are involved in the executive decision-making process. The outcomes of the decisions are a 
function of the people who are involved in making the decisions (Amason, 1996; Amason & 
Mooney, 2008). This measurement is used in research to measure the executive decision-making 
process (Amason & Mooney, 2008). The respondents will be asked to rate the overall executive 
decision-making process and the effect on the organisation. A perceptual measure of executive 
decision-making process will be used, which also have been used in previous studies (Amason, 
1996). Also, Dess and Robinson (1984) proposed that when objective measures are not available, 
perceptual measures of executives are effective substitutes. 
3.8  CRITERIA FOR INTERPRETING THE FINDINGS 
Regarding a case study, there are some validity problems. It is hard to generalize findings 
(external validity). It therefore can be hard to relate back to the theoretical model after the research 
has been conducted. Therefore, a multiple case study can provide more valuable information 
regarding the use of heuristics and rational for companies in general. But to generalize the findings 
of this research can be difficult. However, if the model seems to work fine in this explorative 
research, the model can be applied in other case studies in future research. There are four tests that 
are important to keep in mind when conducting a case study. With a case study, it is much more 
complex that the standard validity and reliability notions to which most subjects have been 
exposed, and each item deserves explicit attention (Yin, 1994). They will be discussed below. 
3.8.1  CONSTRUCT VALIDITY 
The construct validity is about establishing the correct optimal measures for the concepts 
being studied. This test can be problematic in case study research. Yin (1994) argues that 
researchers often fail to develop a sufficiently operational set of measures and subjective 
judgments are often used to collect the data. To meet the test of construct validity it is important 
to cover the following two steps: 1) Selecting the specific types of changers that are to be studied 
(in relation to the original objectives of the study). 2) Demonstrate that the selected measures of 
these changes do indeed reflect the specific types of change that have been selected. 
To establish the first step of construct validity in this study it is important to operationalize 
the chosen heuristic and the associated rational, so that they can be measured in a viable way. The 
second step is justifying why using a questionnaire are the right measures that reflect the constructs 
in a way that the research is viable. 
In case studies, according to Yin (1994), there are three available tactics to increase the 
construct validity: 1) Using multiple sources of evidence in a way that encourages convergent lines 
of inquiry. 2) Establishing a chain of evidence. 3) Have the draft case study report reviewed by 
key informants. 
In this research, multiple interviews will be conducted to create convergence in the multiple 
sources of evidence. Also, the selected heuristic and associated rational decision styles will be 
measured with the use of a questionnaire; this will create a chain of evidence. Before conducting 
the questionnaires, a draft case study will be reviewed by the supervisor of this Ph.D thesis. 
3.8.2  INTERNAL VALIDITY 
The internal validity is especially critical in experimental and quasi-experimental research 
(Cook & Campbell, 1979). Internal validity is only a concern for causal (or explanatory) case 
studies, in which an investigator is trying to determine whether event x led to event y (Yin, 1994). 
This is not the case in this research. However, another main concern regarding internal validity is 
the fact that internal validity may be extended to the broader problem of making inferences. An 
inference arises when an event cannot be directly observed.  
The investigator will thus infer that a particular event resulted from an earlier occurrence, 
based on interview and documentary evidence collected as part of the case study (Yin, 1994). This 
is a particularly important point to keep in mind in this study. This study tries to infer a particular 
event from the past with conducting a questionnaire. It can be extremely hard for people to even 
recognize the use of heuristics and rational now they use them, let alone when questionnaires are 
conducted about how people experienced certain strategic decisions in their organisation. It can be 
hard to achieve internal validity in this research, so operationalizing the construct has to be 
executed in a cautious way. 
3.8.3 EXTERNAL VALIDITY 
The external validity determines how much the domain to which the studys findings can 
be generalized. This test deals with the problem whether the findings of the study are generalizable 
beyond the case study. The external validity problem has been a major barrier in doing case 
studies (Yin, 1994, p. 36). A case study offers a poor basis for generalizing. Case studies rely on 
analytical generalization where the investigator strives to generalize the results of the case study 
to a broader theoretical frame, because of this lack of generalizability of the findings it is important 
to look at the analytical generalizability. (Yin 2010; Tsang, 2013) describes the analytical 
generalizability as follows: 
"With both the case study and the laboratory experiment, the objective for generalizing the 
findings is the same: The findings or results from the case study are to follow a process of analytic 
generalization. Analytic generalization may be defined as a two-step process. The first involves a 
conceptual claim whereby investigators show how their case study findings bear upon a particular 
theory, theoretical construct, or theoretical (not just actual) sequence of events. The second 
involves applying the same theory to implicate other, similar situations where analogous events 
also might occur." (Yin, 2010) 
So, the analytical generalizability consists of two steps. The first step is showing the case 
findings regarding the theoretical part of this research. After that it is important to apply that theory 
to other similar situations where comparable events take place. Therefore, this research has an 
explorative focus, while working in a deductive way. 
3.8.4 RELIABILITY 
Reliability refers to whether an assessment instrument gives the same results each time it 
is used in the same setting with the same type of subjects. Reliability essentially means consistent 
or dependable results. Reliability is a part of the assessment of validity (Sullivan, 2011). Reliability 
is defined as the extent to which test scores are free from measurement error (Muijs, 2011). It is 
a measure of stability or internal consistency of an instrument in measuring certain concepts 
(Jackson, 2003). The reliability of the instrument is measured using internal consistence reliability, 
which is measured by alpha coefficient reliability or Cronbach Alpha, the value of Cronbachs 
alpha must be above 0.7 for the items to be acceptable for the research purposes (Ghazali, 2016). 
A relationship exists between validity and reliability. Any instrument can be reliable but not valid 
however, it cannot be valid if it is not reliable (Jackson, 2003). 
Reliable research demonstrates that the study can be repeated and ending with the same 
results. So, the emphasis is on doing the same case over again and create the same results, it is not 
about doing another case study and arrive at the same results. The goal of reliability is to minimize 
the errors and biases in a study (Yin, 1994, p. 36). The general way of tackling the reliability 
problem is to make as many steps in the operationalization as possible to achieve the same process 
when the research is repeated. 
In conclusion, the four tests can be considered as relevant in judging the quality of a 
research design. Some of the tactics are needed when designing the case study, other tactics are 
occurring during the data collection and analysis. A summary of this analysis of these four criteria 
of assessment of the interpretation of findings is presented in Table 3.3 which shows the approach 
used against each of the four tests.

CHAPTER FOUR: DATA ANALYSIS AND RESULTS 
4.1 INTRODUCTION 
The previous chapter (Chapter 3) reviewed research methodology including criteria for 
judging research design and the associated research methods, population and sample, data 
collection, and data analyses. A quantitative research approach using survey questionnaire and a 
qualitative research approach using interviews as well as different data analysis techniques as 
appropriate to address research questions were used. 
This chapter presents several major topics that include analyses of the survey data collected 
from Qatari different levels of management and data collected by interviews, results of using 
different analysis techniques to include descriptive analysis, reliability tests, correlations, 
multivariate analysis, multiple linear regression, factor analysis addressing five reduced 
components. 
4.2 OPERALISATION OF THE STUDY 
This study uses mainly used quantitative approaches to construct a questionnaire from which to 
establish decision making factors the relationships between the demographic and contextual 
variables to form the main body of results and conclusions. In the event only four qualitative 
interviews were conducted. It was hoped that several more, perhaps up to 20 interviews would be 
conducted, until theory saturation was reached. However, due largely due to the Covid 19 
pandemic it became impossible to conduct more than four interviews and the need for thematic 
analysis using NVivo was reassessed and not implemented. The four interviews essentially were 
used to represent the executive personas that broadly confirmed the first part of the main 
proposition P1: that executives in Qatar use a range of decision-making styles, including rational 
and heuristic styles. 
The use of the interview method is deemed useful in this research. By using the interview, 
this research seeks to enrich the information collected from the secondary data by reviewing the 
literature concerning decision making styles and decision making processes  and the data set 
collected by a quantitative research questionnaire contains scale items used by different 
researchers that were identified from prior theorizing and empirical research and defined in 
behavioural terms according to Scott and Bruce (1995), Dew et al. (2009), Matzler et al. (2007), 
Ballou (1989), and Williams (2010). These items cover all decision-making styles mentioned in 
the research theoretical model. Analyzing the interviewers responses, where to some degree 
thematic analysis and overviews for each executive are used to depict the main ideas that all 
interviewers have the same opinion about them as shown in appendix 10: semi structured 
interviews responses, although data collection was limited by the COVID 19 pandemic (see 
APPENDIX 11) the data revealed the following findings:  
1) All interviewees responses reflect rationality in decision making process and at the same time 
use heuristic elements to enhance quality of decisions. So, both the rational decision-making 
style and the heuristic decision-making style are combined in their decision-making process.   
2) When asked how thoroughly did the executive's group search and analyse information in 
making this decision?  The answers were very thoroughly and comprehensively using analytic 
techniques in making the decision which are especially important to them in the process, 
focusing their attentions on crucial information, ignoring irrelevant information. They all 
expressed the importance of their professional experience as crucial while deliberating for the 
decision and expressed that their primary care is for the organisational needs and see those as 
their personal goals and not purely as organisational needs.  
4.2.1 QUESTIONNAIRE DESIGN AND SAMPLE SELECTION 
In the quantitative approach, a research questionnaire is used where six decision-making 
styles were identified from prior theorizing and empirical research and defined in behavioural 
terms according to Scott and Bruce (1995), Dew et al. (2009), Matzler et al. (2007), Ballou (1989), 
and Williams (2010):  
1) Rational decision-making style is characterized by a thorough search for and logical 
evaluation. 
2) Heuristic decision-making style where heuristics are strategies that ignore part of the 
information with the goal of making decisions faster, more frugally, and/or more accurately 
than more complex methods. 
3) Intuitive decision-making style is characterized by a reliance on hunches and feelings. 
4) Dependent decision-making style is characterized by a search for advice and direction from 
others. 
5) Avoidant decision-making style is characterized by attempts to avoid decision making.  
6) Spontaneous decision-making style where spontaneous decision-makers have a sense of 
urgency and make rapid decisions. 
A pilot study was used to test the questionnaire, in terms of face validity and utility to 
investigate the relationships between different decision-making styles and relevant outcomes. 
Then an on-line Survey Monkey questionnaire was administered and carried out between 
November 2019 to January 2020, using social media outlets (Facebook and WhatsApp). A 
minimum sample size of 200 was consider necessary for principal components analysis given the 
number of items in the questionnaire but not necessarily adequate, which would be determined by 
the Kaiser-Meyer-Olkin (KMO) statistic.  The sampling continued until the KMO was shown to be 
adequate. The KMO was tested at a sample size of 200, 250, 300 and 350. In the event, a sample 
of 350 was shown to be adequate and was eventually completed by 350 Qatari Executive 
Managers, at which point the sampling stopped. Principal components analysis was conducted 
using SPSS software. which produced a measure of the adequacy of the sample using the Kaiser-
Meyer-Olkin statistic, which was 0.601 which showed the sample was adequate for the principal 
components analysis. There are no official statistics detailing how many of the approximately 330,000 
Qatari nationals are executives but ity is estimated that there are at most 10% in executive positions. So 
the 350 would represent over 1% of the entire Qatari national executives.  Participation was 
voluntary encouraged to answer as honestly as possible, and they were assured that their answers 
would remain confidential. The survey questionnaires were in 3 parts:  
1) Demographic questions consist of 4 questions to include age, number of years in the current 
business, highest level of education, and business organisation/unit. Participants were asked to 
choose the answer for each question that best described their situation. 
2) 25 closed questions describe different decision-making styles, taken from Scott and Bruce 
(1995) study, including rational decision-making style, dependent decision-making style, 
avoidant decision-making style, and spontaneous decision-making style (5 questions each), 
and 15 closed questions taken from Dew et al. (2009), Matzler et al. (2007), Ballou (1989), 
and Williams (2010), describe an expert heuristic decision making,  
3) 11 closed dichotomous questions (Yes/No) represent 3 levels of decision making that include: 
1) Decision Making Level 1: Operational decisions which Concern day to day activities, and 
normally involve actions in the short term. 2) Decision-Making Level 2: Managerial decisions 
which concern the allocation and use of resources and normally involve longer term. 3) 
Decision-Making Level 3: Strategic decisions which concern broader organisational issues and 
normally involve the long term.  
Data was prepared and coded in an Excel spreadsheet prior to analyses then pasted into SPSS, 
cleaned for errors, and checked for missing data. Factor analysis (Principal components analysis) 
was used to reduce number of items and to form six components (concepts that group together in 
the respondents minds without the other items that were mixed up in the respondents minds; 
sometimes called fragmented cognitive shrapnel). 
In this study five analysis techniques using SPSS version 23 were used to address the 
research questions. The analysis techniques with the associated research questions are: 1) 
Descriptive analysis. 2) Principal Components Analysis to confirm the six decision-making styles, 
Component 1 is Avoidant decision-making, Component 2 is Heuristic decision-making, 
Component 3 is Rational decision-making, Component 4 is Intuitive decision-making, Component 
5 is Dependent decision-making, and Component 6 is Spontaneous decision-making. 3) one-way 
ANOVA 4) GLM analysis, and 5) Pearson correlations. 
Results and discussions of the results are presented in association with the analysis 
techniques and the research questions. In the discussion section, the results are explained within 
the context of this study as well as prior studies reviewed in Chapter 2. Significance of test results 
is reported in the ways suggested by Alghabban (2001, 2004) based on p the probability level: 
1) Not significant (symbolised as NS): p > 0.05, 
2) Significant (symbolised as *): p < 0.05, 
3) Highly significant (symbolised as **): p < 0.01, and 
4) Very highly significant (symbolised as ***): p < 0.001. 
Alghabban (2001) affirms that a p-value < 0.05 suggests that the probability of the result 
occurring by chance is 1 in 20; whereas, when p < 0.01, the chance is 1 in 100, and likewise if p < 
0.001, the chance is 1 in 1000 (Alghabban, 2001, 2004, p. 397). All probabilities reported are based 
on two-tailed tests as each comparison has two possible directions (Perry, 1998). 
Factor analysis was performed with 1 as the Eigen value. Then, six factors were extracted 
when the rotation converged in their iterations. The first factor (Qs 16, 17, 19) is avoidant decision-
making style where avoidant decision-makers avoid making a decision whenever possible, the 
second factor is (Qs 33, 34, 38) is expert heuristic style, the third factor (Qs 2, 3, 4) is rational 
decision-making style where rational decision-makers conduct an extensive search for alternatives 
and make a logical choice between them, the fourth factor (Qs 6,7) is intuitive decision-making 
style where intuitive decision-makers use their intuition to make decisions, the fifth factor (Qs 13, 
15) is dependent decision-making style where dependent decision-makers rely on others to guide 
their decision-making, the sixth factor is spontaneous decision-making style. The second factor is 
according to (Dew et al. (2009), Matzler et al (2007), Ballou (1989), and Williams (2010), while 
the rest of the factors (1, 3, 4, 5, 6) are according to Scott & Bruce (1995). A MANOVA was 
conducted to see how the six decision-making factors vary according to various managers concerns 
include the following: day to day activities of the business, short term activities, immediate 
payment decisions, the allocation of resources, the use of resources, managers across the 
organisation, long term activities, how many people to deploy on tasks, broad organisational 
issues, top executives, and strategic decisions. 
4.3 ANALYSIS 
4.3.1 DEMOGRAPHICS 
The participants demographics surveyed include age, number of years in the current 
business, highest level of education, and business organisation/unit. This includes executive 
decision makers from all levels of management (strategic, managerial, and operational) from both 
public sector that includes military and government background, and different business of private 
sector from different industries that include oil and gas construction, food industry.  
The following are the descriptive statistics of participants in terms of frequency and 
percentage that include age group in years, highest level of education, number of years in current 
business and business organisation/ unit as shown in Table 4.1 below. 
Table 4.1 highlights the descriptive statistics (frequency) for demographics of the 350 
respondents of executive managers of all levels in Qatar that includes executive decision makers 
from all levels of management (strategic, managerial and operational) from both public sector (241 
respondents) performing roles ranging from different level of military background to different 
level of government employees, and different business of private sector (109 respondents) 
employed to different companies of oil and gas industries, constructions, food industries and others 
working in the market. Results were as follow:  
 Age Group: The respondents with an age of 60 and above were the smallest number (4.6%), 
and respondents with an age 20-29 were the second smallest number (8.2%); in contrast, 
respondents with an age 40-49 were the largest number (36.3%); and respondents with an age 
50-59 were the second largest (26%); followed by those in age 30-39 (24.9%). 
 Highest level of Education: Most respondents in the sample hold postgraduate degrees 
(48.3%); Bachelors degrees (46.9%) and, (4.8%) holding A high National Diploma (HND). 
 Number of Years in Current Business: Respondents working 1-9 years in current business 
were the highest number (39.4%), followed by those working for 10-19 years (26.8%); and 
those working for 20-29 years (22.9%), in contrast (8.6%) working 30-39 years and less (2.3%) 
working 40-49 years in current business.  
 Business Organisation/Unit: Most respondents are working in public sector (68.9%); while 
those working in private sector are less (31.1%). Those respondents from public sector are 
government employed personnel working in different ministries, government institutions and 
military personnel from ministry of defense, and those from private sectors are personnel 
working in different industries and business organisations owned by private entities.

4.4 RELIABILITY TESTS 
The reliability test of the variables related to each factor was conducted using Cronbachs 
alpha, Table 4.8 shows results of Reliability Test under the factors 1,2,3,4, 5 and 6. All Cronbachs 
Alpha results are above .8 except Factor 6 which has 0.647 reliability, where the threshold of 
accepted Cronbachs Alpha is 0.7. Cronbachs alpha is a statistic commonly quoted by authors to 
demonstrate that tests and scales that have been constructed or adopted for research projects are 
fit for purpose (Taber, 2018). A wide range of different qualitative descriptors was used by authors 
to interpret alpha values calculated (Taber, 2018), these descriptors are reported with the range 
representing the highest and lowest values of alpha which were described as excellent (0.930.94), 
strong (0.910.93), reliable (0.840.90), robust (0.81), fairly high (0.760.95), high (0.730.95), 
good (0.710.91), relatively high (0.70 0.77), slightly low (0.68), reasonable (0.670.87), 
adequate (0.640.85), moderate (0.61 0.65), satisfactory (0.580.97), acceptable (0.450.98), 
sufficient (0.450.96), not satisfactory (0.40.55) and low (0.11). The common notion of there 
being a threshold of acceptability for alpha values, if only as a rule of thumb (Plummer et al., 
2015), was not always seen as implying that lower values of alpha should be taken as indicating 
an unsatisfactory instrument. Nunnally (1978) is often associated with the assertion that 
instruments used in basic research should have reliability of 0.70 or better. Taber (2018) Alpha is 
then widely used by authors in science education to represent the reliability, or the internal 
consistency, of an instrument or an instrument scale in relation to a particular sample or subsample 
of a population. These terms are often seen as synonymous in relation to alpha, and a number of 
alternative terms are also associated with alpha values cited in science education.

A value of around 0.70 or greater is widely considered desirable (although characterization 
of the qualitative merits of specific values seems highly variable between studies). Schmitt (1996) 
has suggested that there is no general level (such as 0.70) where alpha becomes acceptable, but 
rather that instruments with quite a low value of alpha can still prove useful in some circumstances. 
Accordingly, the researcher assumes that the values Cronbachs Alphas of the factors variables 
are acceptable.  
4.5 VALIDITY 
Given the natural ordering of the concepts in the collective minds of the executives in the sample, 
that represented all the executives in Qatar and the principal components analysis itself confirms 
the validity of the six decision making components as shown in the rotated factor matrix (Table 
4.12) and of the six scales' abilities to discriminate between respondents (Table 4.15). The items 
in each sub-scale were in the same factor space across the sample of respondents. Thus, the 
decision-making questionnaire is valid for use with Qatari executives but as Knekta et al. (2019, 
page 1) state "validity is not a property of the measurement instrument but rather refers to its 
proposed interpretation and use. Validity must be considered each time an instrument is used 
(Kane, 2016)". When used if other contexts confirmatory factor analysis will then confirm or refute 
the validity of the questionnaire in those contexts. It is also reasonable to assume that the 
executives were motivated and honest when answering the instrument as executives making 
decisions in different contexts were shown to have similar decision-making profiles to other 
executives making decisions in similar contexts.

(0.5) which means that KMO statistic is adequate. Similarly, the Bartletts test sphericity is 0.000 
which is statically significant since its value is less than .001 p-value. Testing the hypotheses, Ho: 
correlation matrix which is Identity Matrix against H1: correlation matrix which is not Identity 
Matrix and based on (Bartletts Test) and Since (p-value) is equal 0.000, then 0 is rejected and 
1 accepted which means that correlation matrix is not the identity matrix.

The correlations vary with a range from -1 to +1.  The reduced number of items shows that 
six components (concepts that group together in the respondents minds) were extracted, as the 
other items are shrapnel and are mixed up in the respondents minds, all questions within each 
component have a strong relation as shown in the table. These components are as follows: 
component 1 is (Qs 16, 17, 19) reluctance to make decisions , Component 2 is (Qs 33, 34, 38) 
balancing the requirements, component 3 is (Qs 2, 3, 4) rational decision making , component 
4 is (Qs 6, 7) intuitive decision making, Component 5 is (Qs 13, 15) co-operative decision 
making, and component 6 is spontaneous decision-making. Table 4.13 is the Total Variance 
Explained. In this table the initial Eigenvalues are the variances of the factors.  Since factor analysis 
is conducted on the correlation matrix, the variables are standardized, so, each variable has a 
variance of 1, and the total variance is equal to the number of variables used in the analysis, which 
is in this case, 15.  
Figure 4.1 shows the eigenvalue against factor number. 6 components were extracted based 
on Eigenvalue greater than 1. The values in the first six columns of Table 4.13 are immediately 
above the Eigenvalue 1, and from the 7th possible component the line starts to flatten, which means 
that each successive factor starts to account for less and less amounts of the total variance. This 
means that just the six factors are relevant and connected in the minds of the respondents. 
Figure 4.1 Scree Plot

which explains a cumulative of 77.987 % of the total variance. Under the Initial Eigenvalues,

the total variance, with a cumulative value 34.906 %, factor 3 explains 12.800 % of the total

value 69.981 %, and factor 6 explains 8.006 % of the total variance, with a cumulative value 77.987 
% (Table 4.13).

As shown in Table 4.14, H02 (there is NO Statistically Significant (0.05) difference 
between decision-making styles of executives and their demographics in Qatar is only partially 
supported. Factor 1 (Avoidant decision-making style) has positive significant correlation with 
number of years in the current business at the 0.05 level, Factor 4 (intuitive decision-making 
style) has positive significant correlation with level of Education at the 0.01 level, and F5 
(dependent decision-making style) has positive significant correlation with both number of years 
in the current business and level of education at the 0.05 level. Age is not significantly correlated 
with any of the decision-making styles.

CHAPTER FIVE: DISCUSSION  
5.1 INTRODUCTION 
This chapter presents the development and preliminary validation of the custom designed 
instrument to assess decision making styles within executive decision makers in Qatar. Six 
decision making styles postulated a priori from the literature were used as independent styles in 
the executive decision makers in Qatar. The chapter then discusses the decision-making 
components and their validity and the application in the model in different business environments. 
Two models are presented and discussed: a) the "Mixed Environment Driven Model Under 
Uncertainty" and b) the "Mixed Environment Driven Model Under Certainty". The applications of 
the research and its implications for policy, practice and theory are also discussed. 
Studying the effects of rational and heuristic models elements on decision making process 
is something not new; but this research is giving set of advantages which make it a holistic study 
especially in promised developing country such as Qatar. Discussing the effects of rational and 
heuristic models on improving decision making process has been discussed and reported in the 
literature (Papamichail & Rajarm, 2007; Francis, 2016; Papula, & Papulova, 2015; Fox, 2015; 
Xiao et al., 2018; Marewski et al., 2010; Hauser, 2011; Thoma & Williams, 2013; Hilbig, 2014; 
Kitjacharoenchai et al., 2019). Most of previous studies agreed that applying rational or heuristic 
model can give the firm more ability to improve the way of making decisions which directly affects 
the firm products and services. In this research, the researcher tried to give more added values 
through entering deeply in the effectiveness of rational model, heuristic model, and their effects 
on the whole decision-making process. 
As it has been found in this research, though, level of education is not a decisive factor in 
developing and applying new advanced model of management. The results support the argument 
that, while education is a prerequisite for management advancement and transformation, it is not 
sufficient, and it does not bring automatic results.  
The results of this research support the idea that, the dominant mode of decision-making 
process in any economy is influenced by many factors, and while it is an indicator of the level of 
advancement in the entire economy, it has its impact on the rate and direction of socio-economic 
growth. In other word, while management has its own impact on the advancement of the economy, 
it is not developed in vacuum (Keating, 2001).  There is a feedback loop between advancement in 
management and other socio-economic factors.     
While management advancement is a tool of socio-economic transformation, there is a 
relation between the sense of need for economic development and transformation in one hand, and 
the development in public management in the other hand. (Indeed, public management reform did 
not develop out of a vacuum. The drive for public management reform was fundamentally a 
response to the increasing dissatisfaction with government performance in maintaining economic 
and social development). (Keating, 2001).  
The case of developing private management is not far from this perspective. It has many 
levels of positive correlation. (The analysis reveals that the positive effects of performance 
management on work effort and innovative behaviour were significant for private sector 
employees), (Lee, 2018). Also, Lee (2018) argues that (performance management influences 
performance outcomes in otherwise similar public and private organisations).  
The profiles of the decision-making styles of the Qatari executives are shown in the box 
and whisker plot of Figure 5.1 derived from the standardised factor data and the means and 
standard deviations shown in Table 5.1 below. 
Figure 5.1 
Decision-making Styles Exhibited by Respondents (Box and Whisker Plot)

Findings from addressing the research questions and by using descriptive statistics show 
that respondents exhibited all decision-making styles at different levels. The correlations showed 
support the notion that the decision-making styles are not mutually exclusive and that individuals 
do not rely on a single decision-making style. The findings in this study indicate that individuals 
use a combination of decision-making styles in making important decisions, which support the 
argument of Scott & Bruce (1995), However, research indicates that individuals, due to a lack of 
time and commitment, tend to rely on shortcuts when making decisions (Payne et al., 1993). 
Consequently, the concept of fast and frugal heuristics has gained attention as an important concept 
to describe human judgments (Gigerenzer et al., 1999). In this study, respondents mostly exhibited 
Expert Heuristic Decision Making style (balancing the requirement) with 27.9% (agree and 
strongly agree responses). 
Rational decision-making is achieved through a long decision-making process because a 
leader seeks significant amounts of information to make an objective and logical decision (DuBrin 
et al.,1989). As a result, rational decision-making style yields a rational decision. In this study, 
respondents exhibited rational decision-making style where Rational decision-makers conduct an 
extensive search for alternatives and make a logical choice between them with 25.72% (agree and 
strongly agree responses). These percentages (27.9% and 25.72%) support the argument of Simon 
(1957) bounded rational model, where the perfect rationality turned into bounded rationality based 
on limited information processing judgment heuristics and satisfying choices. 
On the other hand, 14.82% (agree and strongly agree) of respondents exhibited intuitive 
decision-making style where intuitive decision-makers use their intuition to make decisions. 
However, a leader sometimes makes an intuitive decision (DuBrin et al.,1989) because intuition is 
a real phenomenon and contributes to effectiveness, especially in situations where it counts time-
pressured complex decision-making in the real world (Salas et al., 2010, p. 966), while 18.8% 
(agree and strongly agree) exhibited dependent decision-making style, where dependent decision-
makers rely on others to guide their decision-making. On the questions regarding avoidant 
decision-making style where avoidant decision-makers avoid deciding whenever possible 
Reluctance to make decisions, respondents exhibited 6.1% (disagree and strongly disagree 
responses) and 6.66 % spontaneous. What does that mean? That idicates the fact that Qatar is one 
of the developing countries with huge economic resources that must be used in a professional way, 
effectively and efficiently, so there is no room for avoiding decisions or applying spontaneous 
decision making style, avoidant decision making style is arguably a risk aversion approach to 
decision making which does not provide effectiveness and efficiency when economy work in 
dynamic and uncertain environment rather than to be proactive in taking decisions, at the same 
time taking spontaneous decisions in uncertain, dynamic environment might be too risky. I believe. 
Therefore, respondents exhibited all important decision-making style with high percentage except 
avoidant and spontaneous decision-making style. These finding also supports Spicer & Sadler-
Smith (2005) argument that people were likely to use various decision- making styles despite 
practicing one dominant style.  
In this context, Bavolr & Orosov (2015), found mostly weak, but statistically significant 
relationships between the decision-making styles and decision-making competencies. It was 
mainly the spontaneous and avoidant decision-making styles that correlated negatively with the 
decision-making competencies, and this was also partly true for the intuitive decision-making 
style. While the reported use of the dependent style was weakly and negatively related to decision-
making competence, the only (but again low) significant positive correlation was found in the 
rational style. The more general view using linear regression showed that mainly using the 
avoidant and spontaneous styles is associated with lower decision-making competence. 
The decision-making style a leader displays depends on certain contextual variables. For 
example, when transformational leaders need to make a comprehensive decision by involving 
others to obtain information, they tend to use rational decision-making style. In contrast, leaders 
with transactional and laissez-faire leadership styles tend to produce a less comprehensive 
decision; thus, such leaders are likely to exhibit spontaneous and even avoidant decision-making 
styles (Hariri et al., 2014).  
The nature of todays environment is problematic, dynamic, and uncertain, where 
incomplete and imperfect information a companied with scarcity of resources and time constraints 
are the dominant factors when taking decisions, besides what complicate decision process in terms 
of shifting, ill-defined or competing goals. People trying hard to figure out what to do in the face 
of such difficult circumstances. Governments struggle to face such challenges when the stakes 
often high and the impact of decisions are significant on people lives. The activities involving 
decision making process under these circumstances are complex. When decision making is spoken 
of, this concerns the activities in mind that seeks optimal solutions to the problems. So, it is not 
surprising that substantial efforts have been devoted to understanding the best mechanism to make 
decisions. Accordingly, people that are using their knowledge and experience in coping with 
complex decision tasks are expert decision makers, which does not mean decision analysts, but 
they are familiar with the tools and information sources relevant decision-making. Thus, 
proposition P1 is supported and executives in Qatar have been shown to use a range of decision-
making styles, including rational and heuristic styles, that can be profiled using a structured 
questionnaire. 
When asked, The decisions you make usually concern the following (Please tick "YES" 
to all that apply to you? Q41: Day to day activities of the business, Q42: Short term activities, Q43: 
Immediate payment decisions, Q44: The allocation of resources, Q45: The use of resources, Q46: 
Managers across the organisation, Q47: Long term activities, Q48: How many people to deploy 
on tasks, Q49: Broad organisational issues, Q50: Top executives, and Q51: Strategic decisions. 
These questions represent all decision-making levels which include decision making Level 1 
(operational decisions which concern day to day activities), decision Making Level 2 (managerial 
decisions which concern the allocation and use of resources), and decision-making Level 3 
(Strategic decisions which concern broader organisational issues. As shown in Figure 5.2, 70% - 
94% responses were YES to all concerned Questions, which means that respondents are 
experienced and professional decision-making executives, according to their answers on all levels 
of decision making, and therefore respondents exhibited heuristic, rational, dependent and intuitive 
decision-making styles in that order as shown in Figure 5.1. But the question is, why the YES 
percentage is high on all questions, which concerns all levels of decision making?  The reason 
might be that respondents organisations are highly centralized, and respondents are involved in 
all decision-making levels. 
Figure 5.2 
Yes / No Respondents Answers to Levels of Decision-making 
5.2 ABOUT THE FACTORS 
Principal components analysis was used to reduce number of items to six components 
(concepts that group together in the respondents minds), as the other items are shrapnel and are 
mixed up in the respondents minds. In this study five analysis techniques using SPSS version 23 
was used to address research questions. The analysis techniques with the associated research 
questions are1) Descriptive analysis. 2) Principal components analysis to address six decision-
making styles, Component 1 is Avoidant decision making, Component 2 is Heuristic decision-
making, Component 3 is Rational decision making, Component 4 is Intuitive decision making, 
Component 5 is Dependent decision making, and Component 6 is Spontaneous decision making. 
3) MANOVA and 4) Pearson correlation. 
Results and discussions of the results are presented in association with the analysis 
techniques and the research questions. In the discussion Chapter, the results are explained within 
the context of this study as well as prior studies reviewed in Chapter 2. Significance of test results 
is reported in the ways suggested by Alghabban (2001, 2004) based on p-value the probability 
level: Not significant (symbolised as NS): p > 0.05, significant (symbolised as *): p < 0.05, Highly 
significant (symbolised as **): p < 0.01, and very highly significant (symbolised as ***): p < 
0.001. 
Alghabban affirms that a p-value < 0.05 suggests that the probability of the result occurring 
by chance is 1 in 20; whereas, when p < 0.01, the chance is 1 in 100, and likewise if p < 0.001, the 
chance is 1 in 1000 (Alghabban, 2001, 2004, p. 397). All probabilities reported are based on two-
tailed tests as each comparison had two possible directions.  (Perry, 1998). 
Factor analysis was performed with 1 as the Eigen value. Then, six factors were extracted 
when the rotation converged in their iterations. The first factor (Qs 16, 17, 19) is avoidant decision-
making style where avoidant decision-makers avoid making a decision whenever possible, the 
second factor is (Qs 33, 34, 38) is expert heuristic style, the third factor (Qs 2, 3, 4) is rational 
decision-making style where rational decision-makers conduct an extensive search for alternatives 
and make a logical choice between them, the fourth factor (Qs 6,7) is intuitive decision-making 
style where intuitive decision-makers use their intuition to make decisions, the fifth factor (Qs 13, 
15) is dependent decision-making style where dependent decision-makers rely on others to guide 
their decision-making, the sixth factor is spontaneous decision-making style. The second factor 
(expert heuristic style) is according to Dew et al. (2009), Matzler et al (2007), Ballou (1989), and 
Williams (2010), while the rest of the factors (1, 3, 4, 5, 6) are according to Scott & Bruce (1995). 
When factor analysis is used to reduce number of items to six components (concepts that 
group together in the respondents minds), the following findings were noticed: 
1) Factor 1  Q16, Q17, Q19 (Avoidant decision-making style) where avoidant decision-makers 
avoid making a decision whenever possible, the value of (p-value = 0.000), which means that 
the independent variables (Q16, Q17, Q19) together have a significant (substantial) effect on 
the dependent variable (Factor 1) at a significant level of (  0.05). 
2) Factor 2  Q33, Q34, Q38 (Heuristic decision-making style) where heuristic is a mental 
shortcut that allows people to solve problems and make judgments quickly and efficiently. 
These rule-of-thumb strategies shorten decision-making time and allow people to function 
without constantly stopping to think about their next course of action, the value of (p-value = 
0.000), this means that the independent variables represented by (Q33, Q34, Q38) together 
have significant (substantial) effect on the dependent variable (Factor 2) at a level of 
significance of (  0.05).  
3) Factor 3  Q2, Q3, Q4 (Rational decision-making style) where rational decision-makers 
conduct an extensive search for alternatives and make a logical choice between them, the value 
of (p-value = 0.000), which means that the independent variables (Q2, Q3, Q4) combined have 
a significant (substantial) effect on the dependent variable (Factor 3) at a significant level of 
(  0.05) 
4) Factor 4  Q6, Q7 (Intuitive Decision-making Style) where intuitive decision-makers use their 
intuition to make decisions, the value of (p-value = 0.000). This means that the independent 
variables represented by (Q6, Q7) together have a significant (substantial) effect on the 
dependent variable (Factor4) at the significant level of (  0.05). 
5) Factor 5  Q13, Q15 (Dependent Decision-making Style) where dependent decision-makers 
rely on others to guide their decision-making, the value of (p-value = 0.000), which means that 
the independent variables represented by (Q13, Q15) combined have a significant (substantial) 
effect on the dependent variable (Factor5) at the level of significance of (  0.05). 
6) Factor 6  Q21, Q22 (Spontaneous Decision-making Style) where decision makers made Quick 
and impulsive decisions), the value of (p-value = 0.000), which means that the independent 
variables represented by (Q21, Q22) combined have significant (substantial) effect on the 
dependent variable (Factor 6) at the level of significance of (  0.05).

thinkers were more likely to make a rational decision.

F1 (Avoidant Decision-making Style), F2 (Heuristic Decision-making Style), F3 (Rational Decision-making Style),

Avoidant decision-making style is characterized by attempts to avoid decision-making. 
The negative significant correlation between Factor 1 (Avoidant decision-making style) and Factor 
3 (Rational decision-making style) at the 0.01 level supports the argument of Scott & Bruce (1995) 
and the conclusion of Phillips et al. (1984) that rational decision makers tend to approach rather 
than avoid problems. The avoidant decision-making style was found to be negatively related to 
well-being (Bavolr & Orosov, 2015), also depression was found to be positively correlated with 
the avoidant style, brooding and anxious style and was negatively associated with the vigilant and 
intuitive decision-making styles. In the few studies with employees as participants, Rational (Russ 
et al.,1996, sales-managers) and Intuitive (Sadler-Smith, 2004, managers) styles were found to be 
associated with better job performance and avoidant style (Russ et al.,1996) with poorer job 
performance. 
According to Allwood & Salo (2012), persons scoring high on dependent style have less 
flexibility in planning and accomplishing their decision-making errands because they are 
dependent on other persons in the decision process. This would result in less control over the 
decision process and its outcomes. Dependent styles suggests that dependence on others in 
decision-making can be accompanied by a lower tendency to avoid making decisions. Avoidance 
can be inevitable only with a lack of other people able to provide advice, while a higher reported 
use of the rational style correlated positively with the dependent style (Bavolr & Orosov, 2015), 
which concurs with the findings of two previous studies (Baiocco et al., 2009; Loo, 2000). A 
possible explanation could be that an effort to seek advice from other people is a part of a rational 
process (Bavolr & Orosov, 2015). 
Factor 3 (Rational decision-making style) has positive significant correlation with Factor 
4 (Intuitive Decision-Making Style) at the 0.01 level, A classification scheme for decision-making 
style proposed by Harren (1979) includes two opposing categories regarding information gathering 
and evaluation: intuitive and rational. Therefore, individuals can be classified as consistently 
intuitive or consistently analytical in information gathering and evaluation. However, optimal 
strategic decision making may require both rationality and intuition (Elbanna, 2006; Elbanna & 
Child, 2007; Hitt & Tyler, 1991; Langley et al.,1995). Intuition denotes a vague feeling or sense 
of feeling of pattern or relationships. It is also referred as holistic thinking, immediate insight, 
seeing the answer without knowing how it was reached (Thorne, 1990), or as a technique of 
swiftly retrieving hunks and forms of knowledge molded from previous experience (Seal, 1990). 
Decision-makers might sense patterns, feelings, and objects in seemingly unconnected facts. 
Intuition is often perceived by people as a sudden awareness of information (Ritter, & Dijskterhuis, 
2014; Sauter, 1999). Intuitive managers become impatient with routine, details, or repetition. They 
may reach conclusions too quickly, ignore relevant facts, or follow an inspiration when it is clearly 
bad (Agor, 1984). The factors of intuitive decision-making style consist of experience, knowledge 
and others (Harteis et al., 2008). 
A mixed environment driven model to decision-making process is proposed based on 
research results and findings as depicted in Figure 5.3.  This model supports previous literature on 
decision making styles. Previous decision-making theorists used rational decision style and 
intuitive style as two separate techniques of making decisions. A classification scheme for 
decision-making style proposed by Harren (1979) includes two opposing categories regarding 
information gathering and evaluation which are intuitive and rational. Therefore, individuals can 
be classified as consistently intuitive or consistently analytical in information gathering and 
evaluation. 
Three factors that can increase uncertainty in organisation environment. These factors are 
complexity, dynamism, and richness, on Figure 5.3 As the curve progress to the right, the 
environment becomes more complex, less stable, and poorer, the level of uncertainty increases. 
Environmental complexity is a function of the strength, number, and interconnectedness of the 
specific and general forces that an organisation must manage. 
Figure 5.3 
Mixed Environment Driven Model to Decision-making Processes 
The greater the number, and the greater the differences between them, the more complex 
and uncertain is the environment and the more difficult to predict and control (Child, 1972). 
Environmental dynamism is a function of how much and how quickly forces in the specific and 
general environments of an organisation change over time and thus increase the uncertainty an 
organisation faces (Aldrich, 1979). Environmental richness is a function of the abundance of 
resources available to support an organisations domain, where in rich environments, uncertainty 
is low because of the plethora of resources and no need to compete for them (Starbuck, 1976). The 
certainty is high in simple, stable, and rich environment, but in complex, dynamic and poor 
environment the uncertainty is high.  
Rational Decision-Making Style is characterized by a thorough search for and logical 
evaluation of alternatives (Scott & Bruce, 1995), where rational causes decision makers to be 
obliged to consider several alternative scenarios and probabilities for each alternative before 
making decision (Busari & Spicer, 2015; Oliveira, 2007). According to Dean & Sharfman (1993), 
rationality is the extent to which the decision-making process involves the collection of 
information relevant to the decision, and the reliance upon analysis of this information in making 
the choice. Rational decision-making incorporates critical evaluation of evidence and a structured 
process that requires time and conscious effort (Fitzgerald et al., 2017). Rational procedures help 
decision makers establish relevant decision criteria, identify a comprehensive set of alternatives, 
and evaluate the individual alternatives objectively (Kaufmann et al., 2012). In contrast to intuitive 
decision-making style, rational decision-making style encompass cautious and methodical 
thoughtfulness of all conceivable decision choices (Baird, 1989; Tetlock et al.,1993). Scrutinizing 
the available options by means of rational decision-making style necessitates the decision maker 
to deliberately appraise options and their relevant probable consequences. In addition to being a 
cognisant and thoughtful process, rational decision-making style is largely free from 
ridiculousness and predispositions, and thus results in enhanced decision (Pacini & Epstein, 1999), 
higher work and organisational performance (Busari & Spicer, 2015; Singh, 2014; Smolka et al., 
2018; Uzelac et al., 2016). Rational economic model based on neo-classical economic ideology 
was also governed by logic and rational analysis in decision scenarios (Hendry, 2000). All the 
options are considered, rationally evaluated, and the final decision is based on optimal choices 
(Hellreigel et al., 2001; Mangalindan, 2004).  
The perfect rationalism was challenged with the passage of time. Simon (1957) criticized 
the mean-end sequence and proposed a bounded rational model which is well known as normative 
or administrative model of decision making. Thus, the perfect rationality turned into bounded 
rationality based on limited information processing, judgment heuristics, and satisfying choices. 
Lindbloms (1959) incremental model was also based on limited rationality although these models 
were more flexible than perfect rationalism. The major transition took place when Kahneman & 
Tversky (1979) rejected the legacy of perfect rationality and proposed prospect theory of decision 
making. 
Time constraints, decision speed, and cost of acquiring information restricted theorists to 
make decisions with an adequate amount of information (Buchanan & Connell, 2006), therefore 
decision makers tried to find alternatives to rational theory and this why intuitive decision-making 
style earned much popularity in the recent years. While theorists limited decision-making to either 
rational or intuitive strategies, Andersen (2000) proposed that individuals decision-making styles 
can be identified by assessing these two functional dimensions in conjunction. 
Decision literature depicts that past researchers introduced numerous unique decision-
making styles according to their cultures, contexts, and circumstances, but the shared aspect of the 
research on decision making styles is the consideration of rational and intuitive decision making  
style by almost all of the decision researchers (Arroba, 1977; Harren, 1979; Mitroff  & Kilmann, 1975; 
Phillips et al.,1984; Scott & Bruce, 1995) although they considered both styles as distinctive 
dimensions of human nature. Researchers (Klaczynski, 2001; Stanovich & West, 2000; Sternberg, 
1997) illustrated that the line of distinction between cognitive style, learning style, thinking style, and 
decision-making style is based on the distinction between rational and intuitive approaches. The 
past literature indicates that decision researchers were stick to the assumption that decisions are 
solely based either on rationality or on intuition. Payne & Bettman (2004) suggested that attempts 
should be made to understand the interaction between these two styles instead of considering them 
as separate dimensions to confirm Stanovich & West, (2002) argument that these two styles do not 
stand apart on the poles and are more interactive in nature. 
Scott & Bruce (1995) and Thunholm (2004) both found that innovativeness is inversely 
associated with rational, dependent, avoidant, and spontaneous decision-making styles. That is, 
the less innovative an individual is, the more likely it is that he/she will adopt a rational, dependent, 
avoidant, or spontaneous decision-making style. Conversely, an individuals innovativeness was 
positively related to an intuitive style of decision making, suggesting that more innovative 
individuals are more likely to take responsibility for a decision and approach the problem in a 
manner that is based on experience and intuition rather than analytic procedures. 
The mixed environment driven model to decision making process, this research proposed,
