came to support Rowe & Boulgarides (1992) argument that the distinction and integration in 
rational and intuitive decision-making style are based on individual differences, organisational, 
contextual, and environmental factors, but in a broader prospect that include other decision-making 
styles, heuristic, dependent and avoidant. In todays environment organisations are forced to work 
under uncertainty, where multicultural and diversity of the work forces environment is the 
dominant and the room for individual differences expanded. In the times of emergency, 
complexity, ambiguity, uncertainty, risk, and time pressure, intuitive decision-making style is  more 
appropriate that involves a creative solution based on overall situational recognition instead of 
making a feature-by-feature analysis (Bryant, 2002; Callan & Proctor, 2000), Similarly, in the 
absence of the consensus and in the presence of uncertainty, rational style is inappropriate (Dean 
& Sharfman, 1993), but  Bass & Reggio (2006) suggested that both styles stands on a continuum 
on which an individual leader rates his or her leadership which supported Hammond et al. (1997) 
argument that rational and intuitive style should not be rigidly placed on the ends of a continuum 
instead an individuals relative standing between these two styles should determine his or her style, 
where an intuitive decision maker should share some rationality and a rational decision maker 
should consider some intuition to make superior choices, which support the proposed mixed 
environment driven model to decision making processes of this study. This proposed mixed model 
support the argument of Spicer & Sadler- Smith (2005) that a balanced approach in decision making 
can be more appropriate for effective decision making. So, effective decisions can be made, when all 
decision-making styles proposed by the mixed model are taken into consideration by different levels 
of management in organisations. To illustrate the mixed environment driven model to decision 
making processes, the model will be discussed under uncertain environment first, then under 
certain environment. To do so, the mixed environment driven model is divided to two sub-models 
for the purpose of illustration. The first one is Figure 5.3A (see next page) which illustrate the 
model under uncertainty where the environment is complex, dynamic, and poor. 
According to Jones (2013) General environment of an organisation are the forces that shape 
the specific environment and affect the ability of all organisations in a particular environment to 
obtain resources. These forces are: 1) Economic forces, such as interest rates, the state of the 
economy, and the unemployment rate, that determine the level of demand for products and the 
price of inputs. 2) Technological forces, such as the development of new production techniques 
and new information-processing equipment, influence many aspects of organisations operations. 
3) Political, ethical, and environmental forces influence government policy toward organisations 
and their stakeholders, such as laws that favor business interest, and pressure from 
environmentalists. 4) Demographic, cultural, and social forces, such as the age, education, 
lifestyle, norms, values, and customs of a nations people, shape organisations customers, 
managers, and employees. 
Figure 5.3A 
Mixed Environment Driven Model Under Uncertainty  
Pressures from the organisational environment in which they operate also make 
organisations the favored mode for transforming inputs into outputs. An organisations 
environment is the source of valuable input resources and is the marketplace into which it releases 
outputs. It is also the source of economic, social, and political pressures that affect an 
organisations ability to obtain these resources. Managing complex environments is a task beyond 
the abilities of most individuals, but an organisation has the resources to develop specialists to 
anticipate or attempt to influence the many pressures from the environment. This specialization 
allows the organisation to create more value for the organisation, its members, and its customers. 
Accordingly, an organisation likes to have a steady and abundant supply of resources so it can 
easily manage its domain and satisfy stakeholders. All the forces just discussed cause uncertainty 
for organisations and make it more difficult for managers to control flow of resources they need 
to protect and enlarge their organisational domains. The set of forces that cause these problems 
can be looked at in another way; in terms of how they cause uncertainty because they affect the 
complexity, dynamism, and richness of the environment. As these forces cause the environment 
to become more complex, less stable, and poorer, the level of uncertainty increases as illustrated 
in Figure 5.3A by moving along the curve to the right from simple, stable and rich environment to 
complex, dynamic, and poor environment. 
Environmental complexity is a function of the strength, number, and interconnectedness of 
the specific and general forces that an organisation must manage (Child,1972; Dess & Beard, 
1984). The greater the number, and the greater the differences between them, the more complex 
and uncertain is the environment and the more difficult to predict and control. Complexity can 
increase greatly when specific and general forces in the environment become interconnected, that 
is, when forces begin to interact so their effects on the organisation become unpredictable (Emery 
& Trist1965). The more interconnected the forces in an organisations specific and general 
environments, the more uncertainty the organisation faces. The more complex an organisations 
environment, the greater the uncertainty about that environment. Predicting and controlling the 
flow of resources becomes extremely difficult. 
Environmental dynamism is the degree to which forces in the specific and general 
environments change quickly over time and thus contribute to the uncertainty an organisation 
faces. It is a function of how much and how quickly forces in the specific and general environments 
change over time and thus increase the uncertainty an organisation faces (Aldrich 1979).  An 
environment is stable if forces affect the supply of resources in a predictable way. An environment 
is unstable and dynamic if an organisation cannot predict the way in which the forces will change 
over time, global expansion even makes the environment more difficult to predict and control. An 
organisation in a dynamic, unstable environment will seek ways to make it more predictable and 
so lessen the uncertainty it faces. 
Environmental richness is defined as the range of resources available to support an 
organisations domain (Starbuck, 1976; Dess & Beard, 1984). In rich environments, uncertainty 
is low because resources are plentiful and so organisations need not compete for them. 
Environments may be poor for two reasons: 1) An organisation is located in a poor country or poor 
region of a country; and 2) there is a high level of competition and organisations are fighting over 
available resources (Aldrich 1979). In poor environments, the greater the problems organisations 
face in managing resource transactions. Organisations must battle to attract customers or to obtain 
the best inputs or the latest technology. These battles result in uncertainty for an organisation. In 
an environment that is poor, unstable, and complex, resources are especially hard to obtain, and 
organisations face the greatest uncertainty. By contrast, in a rich, stable, and simple environment, 
resources are easy to come by and uncertainty is low.  
Managing the organisational environment is a crucial task for an organisation. The first 
step is identifying sources of uncertainty and examining the sources of complexity, how rapidly it 
is changing, and how rich or poor it is. An organisation then needs to evaluate the benefits and 
costs of different interorganisational strategies and choose the one that best allows it to secure 
valuable resources (Jones, 2013). 
According to Sauter (1999), intuition helps decision-makers contend with uncertainty and 
complexity, so decision-makers reason, analyse, and gather facts that trigger intuition. If intuition 
leads the thought process in a different direction, decision-makers reason and analyse again to 
verify and elaborate upon it. These additional facts and analyses again trigger intuition, and the 
process repeats. Decision-makers can also start with an intuitive hunch, and then analyse it to 
determine its appropriateness. They can also apply intuition at the end of the process to reveal false 
premises, invalid inferences, and faulty conclusions. More and more people realize that intuition 
is essential to making good and right decisions, particularly for those managers at all levels in an 
organisation who sometimes are under conditions of high uncertainty or little precedent (Agor, 
1984; David, 2009). The factors of intuitive decision-making style consist of experience, 
knowledge, and others (Harteis, Koch, & Morgenthaler, 2008). Normally, most of the people use 
intuition for making decisions in situations of great uncertainty or lack of information (Judge & 
Robbins, 2006; David, 2009). Sinclair & Ashkanasy (2005) found that intuitive decision is 
especially useful in ambiguous situations, particularly for those decision makers in business world 
who have restricted information and they must decide which alternative strategies will benefit the 
most. In general, research has found that intuitive decision makers are likely to be more risk 
seeking, impulsive and have a greater belief in luck, while analytic decision makers tend to be less 
risk seeking and less impulsive (Nygren & White, 2002; Barber, 2005).  
Although both rational and intuitive decision-making styles are related to positive 
outcomes, rational decision-making style is considered appropriate in well-planned, well-
managed, and well-organised conditions where less time pressure, less ambiguity and less risk and 
uncertainty is present. In the opposite conditions, intuitive decision-making style is considered 
suitable (Scott & Bruce, 1995). Therefore, if the rational style serves the practical concerns, the 
intuitive style serves the time concerns. Beside these in-built qualities, intuitive decision makers 
are prone to miss some important information in haste and rational decision makers are more likely 
to involve in too much processing. Consequently, a balanced approach in decision making can be 
more appropriate for effective decision making (Spicer & Sadler- Smith, 2005). When both are 
considered at the same time, effective decisions can be made. Effective managers from different 
levels in organisations opt both rational and intuitive choices (Riaz, 2009), The type of 
environment in which a decision is made has also an impact on the decision-making strategy 
(Brder 2003) a high amount of uncertainty present in the environment seems to increase reliance 
on heuristics like take-the-best (Hogarth & Karelaia 2006; Newell et al., 2003). It is also worth 
mentioning that heuristics are argued to be essential in environments that are challenging or 
impossible to comprehend rationally due to high levels of complexity and uncertainty (Loock & 
Hennen, 2015). Bingham & Eisenhardt (2011), emphasise that heuristics may provide accuracy 
with less effort, especially in the context of unpredictable foreign markets. Similarly, Maitland & 
Sammartino (2015) stress that heuristics enable managers to make sense of highly complex and 
uncertain international environments. Researchers (Andersen, 2000; Hansson & Andersen, 2001) 
suggest that managers employing intuitive decision-making style are more likely to make effective 
decisions when the organisations are under a pressure for change. 
The decisions leaders make vary in risk and uncertainty (Certo et al., 2008). Risky decision 
making can be characterized as a decisional situation with objective or given probabilities. 
Conversely, decisions under uncertainty involve decisional scenarios in which probabilities are 
subjective or unknown. Most of the time important decisions involve uncertainty rather than risk 
(Wu et al., 2004). In the times of extreme uncertainty like crisis and emergencies (ODonnell & 
Osborne, 1988) spontaneous decisions are required 
On the other hand, and according to Scott and Bruce (1995), avoidant style is defined as 
an attempt of avoiding decisions whenever possible. It involves indecisiveness, deferring, evading, 
and postponing decisions and keeping oneself away from decision scenarios. They illustrated that 
people opting this style face difficulties while taking decisional initiatives and they are unable 
when they must act upon their intentions. It is ineffective in nature and outcomes. It is 
ineffectiveness is attributed to deficits in the decision maker regarding awareness about of self and 
the environment (Philips et al.,1984). It is positively connected with external locus of control 
demonstrating that individuals with avoidant style are controlled by the external factors rather than 
their internal control orientation (Scott & Bruce, 1995). Decision researchers (Blais et al., 2003) 
illustrated that people having high personal fear of invalidity (PFI) are reluctant decision makers 
who feel frustrated when errors occur, are uncomfortable regarding the costs of errors, hesitant 
while evaluating alternatives, and mostly delay and postpone decisions. Thus, researchers 
demonstrate that such individuals with high PFI are avoidant decision makers. Based on this 
argument, such individuals are not fit to be effective decision makers in uncertain environment 
where ambiguity, dynamism, and complexity are so high.  
Accordingly, the previous arguments about intuitive and heuristic decision-making styles 
support the argument of the proposed mixed environment driven model to decision making 
processes, emphasising that the more uncertain environment (complex, dynamic and poor 
environment) the more intuition and heuristic, the less rational as illustrated in Figure 5.3A. 
The immediate measures taken by the state of Qatar to confront the June 2017 blockade is 
an example of intuitive decisions that the government entities had taken under huge uncertainty 
politicly, economically, and militarily that the countries involved in the blockade put on Qatar. In 
light of such a situation, the exemplary response came from the state, as it was necessary to take 
immediate measures to confront the repercussions of this unjust blockade and its consequences, to 
include launching five direct shipping lines between Hamad Port, which is Qatars main gateway 
for trade with the world, and a number of ports in the region and abroad to ensure that ship traffic, 
maritime navigation and shipping in the country are not affected by the blockade. It was the first 
direct line linking Hamad Port with Sohar Port and another with Salalah Port in the Sultanate of 
Oman and another direct line with India as well as a line linking Hamad Port with the Turkish city 
of Izmir, as well as a new direct weekly shipping line linking Hamad Port in Doha and Karachi in 
Pakistan. All these measures were taken in uncertain environment using the available information 
in their decisions and the good relationship built by Qatari government with friendly countries 
before crisis. After a year of the blockade, these shipping lines reached 23 shipping lines between 
Hamad Port and most of the countries that have trade relations with Qatar. 
Other measures include the formation of a central compensation committee to receive all 
cases of those affected by the blockade, headed by the Attorney General, with the membership of 
representatives of the Qatari Ministries of Justice and Foreign Affairs, to receive compensation 
claims, by the public and private sectors, and by individuals, in addition to the possibility of 
submitting their claims via the committees website. Under the pressure of the crisis, Qatar starts 
immediately encouraging and stimulating the local industry and the shift from consumer culture 
to product culture, preferring and supporting the national product by placing stickers on it in sales 
outlets and commercial complexes, and the states encouragement for national products by 
purchasing a share of the products for the local market, in addition to facilitating and removing the 
difficulties facing exports, and launching many initiatives such as the initiatives launched by the 
Ministry of Economy and Trade Own a factory in Qatar within 72 hours initiative, which is the 
second initiative of the Single Window project in which 250 investment opportunities were offered 
in the industrial sector besides establishing low-cost storage areas that provide the market with 
storage areas estimated at two million square meters in a fast and competitive price that suit their 
demands, during the years 2018 and 2019.  
On the other hand, Figure 5.3B illustrates the 2nd sub-model of the mixed driven 
environment model to decision making processes under certainty. According to Jones (2013) the 
environment is characterized to be simple, stable, and rich, where in a rich, stable, and simple 
environment, resources are easy to come by and uncertainty is low. An environment is stable if 
forces affect the supply of resources in a predictable way. In rich environments, uncertainty is low 
because resources are plentiful and so organisations need not compete for them. An environment 
is characterized to be simple if the strength, number, and interconnectedness of the specific and 
general forces that an organisation must manage are low. 
Decision-Making Style causes decision makers be obliged to consider alternative scenarios 
and probabilities for each alternative before making decision (Busari & Spicer, 2015; Oliveira, 
2007). According to Dean & Sharfman (1993), rationality is the extent to which the decision-
making process involves the collection of information relevant to the decision, and the reliance 
upon analysis of this information in making the choice. Rational decision-making incorporates 
critical evaluation of evidence and a structured process that requires time and conscious effort 
(Fitzgerald et al., 2017). Rational procedures help decision makers establish relevant decision 
criteria, identify a comprehensive set of alternatives, and evaluate the individual alternatives 
objectively (Kaufmann et al., 2012). In addition to being a cognizant and thoughtful process, 
rational decision-making style is largely free from ridiculousness and predispositions, and thus 
results in enhanced decision (Pacini & Epstein, 1999). 
Most of the managers try to reduce uncertainty and ensure certainty to make a better 
decision (Greenberg & Baron, 1993). The uncertainty can be changed into certainty by collecting 
relevant informational and knowledge (Griffin, 2011). Therefore, knowledge management has 
received much attention in the recent years although its existence can be traced back in the history 
(Kucza, 2001). Bryant (2003) illustrates that in three core processes of knowledge management 
including creating, sharing and exploring knowledge, leadership plays a central role. However, 
transformational leaders are better at creating and sharing knowledge at individual and group level 
whereas transactional leaders are more competent in creating and sharing knowledge at 
organisational level. Beside the considerable importance of knowledge management, it is worth 
noticing that it is the responsibility of the leaders to share knowledge and enables subordinates in 
managing knowledge in the organisations and making knowledge-oriented rational decisions 
(Holowetzki, 2002). Transformational leaders use multiple decision-making strategies across 
different situations. Transformational style positively predicted rational, intuitive, and spontaneous 
style. Just like leadership styles, decision making situations directly affect decision making styles, 
in the same manner, certainty, risk and uncertainty directly predicted rational, intuitive and 
spontaneous style respectively (Riaz, 2015). 
Figure 5.3B 
Mixed Environment Driven Model Under Certainty  
Sadler-Smith (2008) asserted that human decision has an intertwined aspect of both 
intuition and deliberation. More generally, complex information processes depend on faint signals 
from the brain which results in considerations (Tranel et al.,1994). Further when these faint signals 
are no longer faint but strong enough to break individuals awareness threshold, it becomes an 
intuition (Becker, 2004). It is important to note that the choice of decision-making style is premise 
on the nature of the problem and the atmosphere. For example, some problems require the use of 
effortful information, deliberation, and precise rule to make decision. Other problems demand no 
predefined rules to arrive at solution. This exertion is the focal quality that differentiates intuitive 
from rational decision-making style (Dijksterhuis & Nordgren, 2006; Kahneman, 2011).  
Dependent decision-making style is attributed to the lack of self and environmental 
awareness (Blustein & Phillips, 1988). People exhibiting dependent style are occupied with 
difficulties in operations related to decision making processes involving deliberate thinking. With 
avoidant style, people find trouble while taking initiatives in decisional scenarios. They are unable 
to practice their attentions when decisional action has to be taken (Scott & Bruce, 1995). 
Rational processes have been a central part of strategic decision-making and a great amount 
of theoretical and empirical research has been conducted on the topic (Elbanna & Child, 2007). 
The higher the strategic level of the problem, the higher the rational content of the decision-making 
process becomes (Krabuanrat 1997). Decision makers identify the best decision to take by 
computing, with perfect accuracy, how different decision alternatives will play out. They choose 
the alternative that maximizes the value of outcomes to them (Berger, 1999). Rational decision 
theory relies on an extensive use of logic and mathematical models to represent decision situations. 
The strength of these rational approaches to decision-making is in their rigor. Working within the 
decision theoretic model allows one to identify answers and weigh the alternatives within the 
model. These approaches encompass a substantial amount of educational content that is straight 
forward to teach and to test (Smith, 2003; Francis, 2016).  
As the curve progresses to the left in Figure 5.3B, the environment becomes simple, stable, 
and rich, the level of certainty increases, and the dominant decision-making style becomes the 
rational with less use of other styles. An example of rational decisions that Qatari government had 
taken under good certainty, politicly, economically, and militarily, its mediation efforts. The small 
state of Qatar has both garnered significant attention and generated considerable controversy 
through its pursuit of several high-profile conflict mediation efforts.  
Mediation is defined by the United Nations as a process whereby a third party assists two 
or more parties, with their consent, to prevent, manage or resolve a conflict by helping them to 
develop mutually acceptable agreements, has long been a key component of international 
relations (United Nations, 2012). While international relations theory has long acknowledged the 
role that small states play in mediation, state-led mediation is often seen as the domain of so-called 
great powers, such as the United States and Russia, which are able to deploy hard power and 
financial might to secure and maintain agreements (Ingebritsen et al., 2012) Many small states, in 
contrast, have built firm reputations as mediators by facilitating dialogue between parties 
(Hoffman & Carment, 2013). Within the Middle East, Qatar has mediated a number of high-profile 
conflicts, bringing it unparalleled attention. Some of the conflicts Qatar attempted to mediate 
include those affecting Lebanon, Palestine, Yemen, Sudan, Djibouti, Eritrea, Morocco, and 
Mauritania (Barakat, 2014).  Qatars mediation efforts have come as part of the states attempts to 
brand itself while boosting its global reputation. A few factors have aided Qatar in translating these 
various motivations into actual mediation efforts. Among these factors, the highly active foreign 
policy, one that sought to raise Qatars profile while balancing between competing ties and 
maintaining open lines of communication with all parties. These contacts helped the country gain 
acceptance as a mediator. At the same time, Qatar possessed the financial resources to transport 
and host large delegations for extended periods of time and build credibility through extensive 
humanitarian work and pledges of investment to support eventual peace agreements. Most 
importantly, a highly personalized decision-making structure allowed a small number of key 
individuals, especially the former emir and former foreign minister/prime minister, to initiate 
mediation efforts and leverage their personal contacts and charisma to secure agreements (Barakat, 
2014).   
A rational theory of mediation must start with a rational theory of conflict because there is 
no mediation without conflict. Conflict can constitute a rational strategy that is preferred to a 
negotiated agreement owing to 1) incomplete information or incentives to misrepresent 
preferences; 2) commitment problems where a negotiated settlement lacks stability because at least 
one state has an incentive to defect; or (3) issue indivisibilities where some issues do not admit 
compromise (Fearon, 1995). Yet, mediators may have access to more information than either 
disputant or possess sufficient resources to change disputants preferences. Thus, they might be 
able to accomplish what the parties themselves are unable to. Accordingly, to succeed as a 
mediator critical information, time availability and enough resources are essentials for excellent 
rational planning and decision-making processes, where the rational decision-making style need. 
The previous arguments about rational decision-making style support the argument of the 
proposed mixed environment driven model to decision making processes, emphasising that as the 
curve progresses from right to left on Figure 5.3B certainty will increase, the more certain 
environment (simple, stable, and rich environment) the more rational, the less heuristic and 
intuition, as illustrated in Figure 5.3B. 
To summarize on the mixed environment driven model for decision making processes, 
progressing to the right along the curve, the more environment uncertainty there will be, the more 
intuition and heuristic and the less rational will be needed, and as the curve regresses right to the 
left on the more environment certainty there will be, the more rational the less heuristic and 
intuition is need as illustrated in Figure 5.3. The overviews of the four executive cases (below) 
broadly support and illustrate how the complexity of different environments tend to different 
decision-making profiles illustrated in Figures 5.3, 5.3A. and 5.3B. 
Overview Interview Case Study 1 
Stable Environment = Rational Decision-Making 
Executive 1 works in Educational and instructional Development profession with part-time 
administration assignment. The decision-making environment he works in has a bounded 
rationality with functional and divisional structure where the environment is stable with moderate 
uncertainty. The company follows a rational decision-making style with a thorough and 
comprehensive processes along with heuristic elements when needed, putting bottom-up plans 
into action to meet fixed strategic goals to seize on opportunities for growth and development. 
Decisions are made at the various divisional levels and are cascaded down to units. A successful 
strategic decision represents the restructuring of units goals and objectives to meet international 
standards but focus on local needs. 
Overview Interview Case Study 2 
Mixed Environment (Stable with Unstable Elements) = Conflict Avoidance and Mixed 
Decision-Making Style 
Executive 2 works in the Higher Education sector with responsibility for staffing, 
curriculum, and resources. The decision-making environment has a bounded complexity with fixed 
structures for governance and oversight. The internal environment is relatively stable and unstable 
situations are avoided whenever possible, although complexity arises when decisions must be 
implemented. Then, conflict is avoided and set procedures are followed. Heuristics may be used 
but within a set of analytic (rational) framework for analysis such as a strengths-weaknesses-
opportunities-threats analysis with progression towards goals being monitored and measured. In 
this sector when there are few complexities then intuition may be used. The executive moved to 
consolidate collaboration and partnership opportunities with key stakeholders, in order to 
minimized any potential types of conflict. There was a conscious effort to focus on the vision, 
mission and objectives to guide and harmonize processes. That is illustrated using the Blue 
Ocean Strategy (Kim and Mauborgne, 2014 
) which is the simultaneous pursuit of differentiation and low cost to open up a new market 
space and create new demand. It is about creating and capturing uncontested market space, thereby 
making the competition irrelevant. It is based on the view that market boundaries and industry 
structure are not a given and can be reconstructed by the actions and beliefs of industry players.  
Blue Ocean Strategy (2022). 
Overview Interview Case Study 3. 
Dynamic Environment = Mixed Approach to Decision Making 
Executive 3 works in the Higher Education sector with responsibility for research and 
planning. The working environment is dynamic where new universities were being established and 
research funding is changing every year without clear direction. As a director of research and 
planning development and based on the evolving environment a mixed of rational, heuristic and 
intuition decision making styles are used in decision making processes. This executive actively 
scanned the external environment and in particular visited universities in Saudi Arabia and Oman 
to understand their general research governance and funding schemes. He also actively 
developed relationships with industrial stakeholders. The focus was to draw on regional 
comparisons rather than wider international issues to move towards the organisations vision. 
Much information and data are considered by the senior management team but as required the 
executive uses his authority to move in a certain direction. 
Overview Interview Case Study 4. 
Stable Environment but Pushing to Maintain World Leading Position = Using Expert 
Heuristics within Rational Frameworks 
Executive 4 works in the Health Sector (in a specialist hospital that is considered world-
leading), specializing in sports medicine and exercise science, with the responsibility of hospital 
administrative matters such as strategic planning, communication management, financial planning, 
and analysis, includes working for critical to check on project implementation and business 
performance. The executive regards himself as a responsible leader that build trust, shares 
employees values and listens to drive engagement. The working environment is stable but a 
mixed of rational, heuristic and intuition decision making styles are followed in decision making 
processes. Strategic decisions are taken in accordance with organisational mission, and vision, 
patient experience and safety and as per the Authority Matrix. The authority matrix is a tool for 
empowerment and role clarification but can also be used for delegation and empowerment. There 
are guidelines on using an authority matrix, which may be thought of as heuristics. Organized 
Change (2022). Additionally, the hospitals vision and mission is directly aligned with the national 
decree issued first in 2012, on the directive of HH Sheikh Tamim bin Hamad Al Thani, the Deputy 
Emir, and Heir Apparent that the second Tuesday of February will be celebrated as Qatar's 
National Sports Day to promote a hearty lifestyle and healthy living among the Qatari population 
(Regency Holidays, 2022). 
5.3 DEFINING EXTERNAL AND INTERNAL CONCURRENT VALIDITY 
Internal validity refers to the degree of confidence that the causal relationship being tested 
is trustworthy and not influenced by other factors or variables. External validity refers to the extent 
to which results from a study can be applied (generalized) to other situations, groups, or events. 
Validity explains how well the collected data covers the actual area of investigation (Ghauri & 
Gronhaug, 2005). Validity basically means measure what is intended to be measured (Field, 
2005). 
Before assessing the validity questions, it is important to understand the goals of a case 
study and the types of errors that can occur when performing the questionnaire. The two main 
goals of a questionnaire are to minimize error in the collected data and measuring the error that is 
part of the questionnaire (Fowler, 2009). Fowler (2009) also defines two fundamental premises. 
The first premise of the questionnaire process is to describe the sample of people who respond, 
can describe the target population. The second premise of a questionnaire research process is that 
the answers of the respondents can be used to accurately describe the characteristics of the 
respondents. It is therefore important to create a sample which is representative for the bigger 
population, which in this case are managers who are involved in strategic decision-making 
processes. 
To confirm the validity of the Questionnaire Pearson Correlations coefficients were found 
by correlating each item questionnaire scores with the score total and based on significant value 
obtained by the Sig.2 tailed of 0.000 < 0.05, it can be concluded that all items are valid, and the 
questionnaire is valid. 
5.4 THE RESEARCH QUESTIONNAIRE 
On-line Survey Monkey questionnaire was administered and carried out between 
November 19 and January 2020 and completed by 350 Qatari Executive Managers.  Participation 
was voluntary encouraged to answer as honestly as possible, and they were assured that their 
answers would remain confidential. The survey questionnaire contains the following:  
1) Demographic questions consist of 4 questions to include age, number of years in the current 
Business, highest level of education, and business organisation/unit. Participants were asked 
to choose the one number for each question that best described their situation. 
2) 25 closed questions describe different decision-making styles, taken from Scott and Bruce 
(1995) study, including rational decision-making style, intuitive decision making style, 
heuristic decision making style, dependent decision-making style, avoidant decision-making 
style and spontaneous decision-making style, and 15closed questions taken from Dew et al. 
(2009), Ballou (1989), and Williams (2010), describe expert heuristic decision making style, 
besides 10 closed questions (Yes No) represent 3 levels of decision making that include 
Decision Making Level 1. Operational decisions: Concern day-to-day activities. Operational 
decisions normally involve actions in the short-term / Decision-Making Level 2 (Managerial 
decisions) that Concern the allocation and use of resources which normally involve longer 
term, and Decision-Making Level 3 (Strategic decisions) that concern broader organisational 
issues, which involve the long term.  
3) Using Likert scale as follows: 1 Strongly Disagree. 2 disagree, 3 Neither Agree nor Disagree, 
4 Agree, and 5 Strongly Agree. participants were asked to answer 40 items concerning the six 
decision-making styles developed by Dew et al. (2009), Ballou (1989), and Williams (2010), 
by choosing the one number for each question that came closest to reflecting their opinion on 
whether they agreed or disagreed with each statement. Besides 10 (YES NO) questions 
regarding decision making levels that include Operational decisions, Managerial decisions, and 
strategic decisions.   
Data were prepared and coded prior to analyses then entered into SPSS, cleaned for errors, 
and checked for missing data. Finally, factor analysis was used to reduce number of items to five 
components (concepts that group together in the respondents minds), as the other items are 
shrapnel and are mixed up in the respondents minds. 
5.5 GENERALISING THE METHODOLOGY 
The definition of generalization is closely related to the concept of external validity, which 
concerns whether findings of one study, and indeed the factor analytic scale used in this study can 
be applied to unexamined subjects and contexts. Generalization pertains to various aspects of a 
research design, including participants, settings, and measurements.  
According to (Best, et al, 2001) causal inferences to the general population from analyses 
of Internet samples necessarily rest on two untested assumptions: (a) that the decision-making 
processes of internet users are like those used by the general population, and (b) that representative 
samples of Internet users can be drawn. The authors provide mixed support for these assumptions. 
They find that current Internet sampling techniques only permit the generation of diverse, not 
representative, samples. However, comparing samples drawn simultaneously using the Internet 
and probabilistic telephone methods, the authors demonstrate that the psychological mechanisms 
underlying common political decisions do not differ between Internet users and the population. 
They discuss the implications of these findings for future survey research. Although Christensen 
& Johnson (2012) found that qualitative researchers view the social world as being dynamic and 
not static. In view of this, they limit their findings to the group of people being studied instead of 
generalizing (De Vaus, 2014). However, here the results of this study are likely to be generalisable 
and the questionnaire would be valid for use in other settings, due to the following: 
1) Social media outlets were used to distribute the questionnaire including Facebook, WhatsApp 
besides e-mails, the sample was representative of the executives and 
2) 40 items of the questionnaire were taken from different published articles and they were re-
evaluated in aa different context in this study. 
5.6 DISCUSSION OF THE MODEL 
Since the research topic is concerning integrating the rational and heuristic elements in 
executive decision-making process in Qatar and its the first in its nature in Qatar, the researcher 
found it important to incorporate all possible decision-making styles in the theoretical model. The 
theoretical model in this case study was developed by consulting literature in this domain as shown 
in Figure 1.2.  It was intended to include in this theoretical model the six common decision-making 
styles developed by researchers to assess the trends in responders minds of executive decision 
makers in Qatar by using different decision-making styles items developed by Scoot & Bruce 
(1995), Harren (1979), Dew et al. (2009), Ballou (1989), and Williams (2010) towards the process 
of decision making in public and private sectors in Qatar. The proposed model includes Rational, 
Intuitive, Dependent, Avoidant, Spontaneous, and Expert Heuristic.  Dependent decision makers 
project responsibility for decisions onto others, in contrast, both rational and intuitive decision 
makers assume personal responsibility for decisions affecting them (Harren 1979). Avoidant 
decision-making style is characterized by attempts to avoid decision making (Scott & Bruce, 
1995). Spontaneous decision-making style is characterized by a sense of immediacy and desire to 
complete the decision-making process as soon as possible (Bavolr & Orosov, 2015). Heuristics 
are rules of thumbs for problem solving (March, 1994). The theoretical model developed in this 
study can benefit future researchers in Qatar to take different approaches to fill gaps in this domain 
and recommend decision styles that fit Qatar environment. 
5.7 WHERE THE RESEARCH WAS UNDERTAKEN 
This case study was undertaken in Qatar. Qatar is a small country located in Southwest 
Asia as shown in figure 5.4. its area is 11,437 sq. km with 
a population of 2.7 million, of whom less than 10 percent 
are nationals. Qatar is also geographically located between 
two large neighbors: Iran, with which Qatar shares the 
worlds largest gas field (North Field, also known as the 
North Dome or South Pars, in the Persian Gulf), and Saudi 
Arabia, with which it has its sole land border, to the south. 
The rest of its territory is surrounded by Gulf waters 
(Baabood, 2017).  Its location in the center of the Gulf 
region and near the major petroleum deposits is 
strategically important. Qatars capital Doha is situated on the east coast of the country. Overall, 
the terrain is almost flat, and the desert is barren (CIA, 2016). Qatar constitutional emirate divided 
to 8 municipalities: Doha, Al Khor and Dhakhirah, Al Wakrah, Al Rayyan, Ash Shamal, Al 
Daayen, Al Sheehaniya and Umm Salal.  Qatar climate can be described as arid, winters are mild 
and very pleasant, summers are in contrast very hot and humid. The mean monthly temperature 
varies between 17 C in January and 36 C in July. In the summer, temperatures above 40 degrees 
are not uncommon. If it rains (which happens infrequently and mostly in winter months), the rain 
falls in brief showers (QTA, 2015). Haze, dust and sandstorms are common occurrences (CIA, 
2016). 
There are only few renewable natural water resources in Qatar, which are used mostly for 
agriculture and private consumption (Mohtar, 2016). Qatars freshwater deficit is addressed by 
desalinated sea water. Desalination plants have high energy demand. However, Qatars domestic 
water use is among the highest in the world (Mohtar, 2016, p. 297). 
The total population of Qatar amounts to 2,660,788 February 2021 published by the 
Planning and Statistics Authority (PSA, 2021). The State is home to people from all over the world 
resulting in the occurrence of many different ethnic groups. Over the years, Qatari nationals 
became a minority in their own country with just 12 percent of the total population (Snoj, 2013). 
In line with this, while the official language of Qatar is Arabic, English is the primary language 
used for communication in the country. 
According to the constitution (Art. 1), Qatars religion is Islam and Sharia law shall be a 
main source of its legislations. Key point in that formulation is that Sharia is a source, and not 
the one source (Tok et al., 2016, p. 8). Moslem religion is a source of the state of Qatar, but not 
the only one. 
Qatar is a monarchy, which is ruled by the tribally based elite Al Thani family, originating 
from the Maadid clan of the Banu Tamim tribe (Gause, 1994) and whose governmental system is 
based on the separation of powers (Hukoomi, 2016b). The Emir is the Head of State of Qatar 
(Hukoomi, 2016a). In 2013, the former Emir Sheikh Hamad bin Khalifa Al Thani, who has 
embarked on an ambitious program of modernizing the country (Kamrava, 2009, p. 401), passed 
the power to his son Sheikh Tamim bin Hamad bin Khalifa Al Thani. This was the first time in 
Qatars history that a leader voluntarily retired from the position of Emir. The change in political 
leaders did not alter the structure of high-level decision making, but what has differed since the 
20112013 period is the larger regional and international contexts within which Qatari domestic 
and international policies are formulated, executed, and, importantly, perceived (Kamrava, 2015, 
p. xii). Together with the legal heir the Emir constitutes the executive authority of Qatar. The 
legislative authority is vested in the Advisory Council and the judicial authority in courts of law. 
Set under the authority of the Emir, the Defense Council supervises the armed forces (Hukoomi, 
2016b). A new permanent Constitution became effective on June 8, 2014 (Hukoomi, 2016c). As 
determined by this Constitution, the Council of Ministers supports the executive authority. It 
consists of the Prime Minister and six supreme councils. The Prime Minister is the head of the 
Council of Ministers and is appointed by the Emir just like all other ministers (Hukoomi, 2016b).  
Qatar is a small, open, well monetized economy with no interest rate ceilings on deposits, 
and where market forces determine the interest rates. It does not have a demographic trap, poverty 
trap, or a savings trap like some of the other developing countries. Since the mid-1990s, the 
continuous efforts by policy makers to diversify revenues, control public expenditures, and 
privatization have led to the improvement of productivity and enhanced long-term economic 
growth (El-Mefleh et al., 2008). 
Qatars economy is one of the richest economies in the world based on GDP per capital, 
ranking between fifth and seventh on world rankings for 2015 and 2016 data compiled by the 
World Bank, United Nations, and IMF. Petroleum and natural gas are the cornerstones of Qatars 
economy and account for more than 70% of total government revenue, more than 60% of gross 
domestic product, and roughly 85% of export earnings. Qatar has the worlds third largest proven 
natural gas reserve and is the second-largest exporter of natural gas. Despite turbulent oil prices in 
the couple of last years the countrys economic growth remained stable. Government efforts to 
diversify incomes and globally invest reserves supported the GDP. The economy is also showing 
resilience and despite the ongoing blockade had an estimated growth of 2.6% in 2017. 
The oil industry, which is owned by the government, is responsible for the power possessed 
by the state. Consequently, the decisions on how to spend oil revenues and who will profit from it 
and by how much is up to the rulers of Qatar. Thus, with oil and gas becoming the driving force 
of economy, the corporate identity and power of the ruling family of Qatar grows. Citizens are 
employed by the political elite and receive financial benefits from them. Because of that the 
citizens are tied to the rulers and are compelled to approve of the way of governance if they strive 
to succeed (Gause, 1994). 
Qatars pre-oil economy was dominated by the pearl industry; however, in the 1930s, 
demand for pearls collapsed due to the introduction of cultured pearls (Ulrichsen, 2016, p. 23). 
Production of oil in the Gulf area began in 1933 in Bahrain; in 1939, oil reserves in Qatar were 
discovered. Qatars economy can be characterized as state capitalism (Ulrichsen, 2016, p. 68), 
as nearly all-important oil and gas producing companies (as, for instance, Qatargas and Qatar 
Petroleum) as well as other non-oil/gas-based firms as Qatar Airways (transportation), Industries 
Qatar (heavy industry), Qatari Diar (real estate) or Ooredoo (telecommunication) are entirely state-
owned (Ulrichsen, 2016, p. 71). The oil and natural gas sector of Qatar is responsible for over 50 
percent of the Gross Domestic Product (GDP) in 2016 (The Heritage Foundation, 2016). The 
countrys reserves of gas are the third largest in the world (CIA, 2016) and in 2007, the arising 
businesses made Qatar even to the top liquefied natural gas (LNG) exporter of the world (Alraouf, 
2012).  
Due to the small population that lacks the necessary skills to drive Qatars economic 
growth, the countrys development depends largely on foreign workers (Moini et al., 2009). Due 
to the increasing dependence on foreign workers, the government introduced a plan to counteract 
this phenomenon. The so-called Qatarization, a concept that requires that a certain percentage 
of Qataris must be employed in private-sector companies, was introduced by the Qatari Labor Law 
of 1962 and mentioned again in 2004 (Zakhidov, 2015). This strategy was implemented by the 
country to bring Qataris into professional and managerial jobs especially in the private sector 
which are currently occupied by expatriate workers. The government focusses strongly on the 
private sector because only a really few percent of all employed nationals work within the truly 
private sector (Moini et al., 2009; Powell, 2014). 
According to the National Vision 2030, one of the most important developments in Qatar 
is that of a world-class educational system that equips citizens to achieve their aspirations and to 
meet the needs of Qatars society, including: educational curricula and training programs 
responding to the current and future needs of the labor market, high quality educational and 
training opportunities appropriate to each individuals aspirations and abilities, and accessible 
educational programs for life-long learning (GSDP, 2008). 
5.8 QUESTIONING THE RESEARCH 
This non-experimental, descriptive study explored the perceptions of executive decision 
making in the minds of executive decision makers in Qatar in both public and private sector 
regarding integrating decision-making styles in decision making process and whether these styles 
is considered credible and usable; Data were collected from a 350 executives survey. This case 
study discussing integrating decision-making styles in decision making process is the first in its 
nature in Qatar. At late stage of this case study, the researcher noticed prospects that can be looked 
at in future for further study.  This issue is related to how to classify the population of this case 
study? Is it homogeneous or heterogeneous population different in its characteristics  and specialty? 
That reflects also on sampling, which entails cluster sampling. To this end also each level of 
management as distinct levels that entails the three levels of sampling for analysis should be 
considered.  
Some weaknesses were identified which come from some of the difficulties encompassed 
sampling. Lack of published literature on the topic of decision-making styles in Qatar made it 
difficult to benefit in assessing the gaps in literature where you can build on that and fill the gaps, 
this lack of studies limits the researchers ability to compare with other studies of the same caliber. 
The other issue in this regard is the difficulties of developing cluster samples to reflect 
different specialization in government and private institutions in Qatar which made it hard to 
pursue cluster sampling which provide a good representation to the whole population.  
Another environmental issue is coronavirus pandemic (COVID-19), which had engulfed 
the nations of the world since started which imposed huge restrictions on movement and official 
meetings, this in turn made it so difficult to administer good qualitative interviews to gain more 
knowledge regarding the process of decision-making of executive decision makers in both public 
and private institutions in Qatar.  
5.9 APPLICATIONS OF THE RESEARCH 
This research can help in enhancing decision-making process in both public and private 
sectors in Qatar. Qatar is one of the promising developing countries with growing economy. This 
case study can be a base for future study that entails many benefits to include making better 
decisions that can best use time and make better use of resources, and at the same time make 
employees to contribute more effectively and be more committed. Using this research and its 
findings can enhance professional development, employee satisfaction and improved engagement. 
This research can be used for further development in future research to fill the gaps and developing 
new theoretical models in the domain.  
5.10 IMPLICATIONS FOR POLICY AND PRACTICE 
Policy is a relatively stable, purposive course of action followed by an actor or set of actors 
in dealing with a problem or matter of concern. Public policies are developed by government 
bodies and officials, Public Policies are those things that governments do and respond to policy 
demand, so it is goal-oriented actions developed through courses or patterns of action over time. 
Policies entails different categories that include:  
1) Constituent policies that involve the composition of government and might be structural; what 
government looks like or Procedural; what government does. 
2) Distributive policies and redistributive policies that entail allocation of services or benefits to 
segments of population. 
3) Regulatory policies which restrict or limit certain behaviours and self-regulatory policies 
which also restrict behaviours but are controlled by the regulated group. 
Public policy decision-making refers to actions taken within governmental settings to 
formulate, adopt, implement, evaluate, or change policies. These decisions may occur at any level 
of government. Policymaking is Lots of decisions (routine or not) over an extended period, and 
decision-making involves making a discrete choice among alternatives at one point in time.  
So, this case study lays the ground for debating this topic related to decision making style 
and set the road by government to establish certain polices that contribute positively in enhancing 
decision making process in both public and private sectors that in turn enhance quality of decisions 
in all levels of leadership. 
5.11 IMPLICATIONS FOR THEORY 
The contributions to theory coming from this research are multifold and can be summarized 
as follows: 
1) Providing a comprehensive and critical review of literature related to decision-making process. 
The literature review conducted in this research is comprehensive and critical. Thus, it is useful 
for researchers and other stakeholders interested in this domain. This is because such a review 
can be used by other researcher to understand what has been done in this domain and what has 
been left behind. This significant in showing the gaps that can be fulfilled to improve the 
related body of knowledge.  
2) Many researchers discuss the subject of this thesis but the differences between them all lies on 
the elements of research and the expansion which make this research consider a fuller study 
including (Papamichail & Rajarm, 2007; Francis, 2016; Papula, & Papulova, 2015; Fox, 2015; 
Xiao et al., 2018; Marewski et al., 2010; Hauser, 2011; Thoma & Williams, 2013; Hilbig, 
2014; Kitjacharoenchai et al., 2019). Thus, future researchers can start to make some 
comparisons between the results of this research with other research. 
3) The primary questions which this research has addressed were related to examining the 
following main points: the first, entering deeply in the usage of rational and heuristic elements 
and how it could be effective on the Executive Decision-making in Qatar. The second, defining 
best practices of the usage of rational and heuristic elements during executive decision-making. 
The third, examining the relation of using the rational and heuristic elements with executive 
decision-making Process. The fourth, choosing the best decision-making styles that executives 
in Qatar mostly exhibit as perceived by respondents, and the fifth conclude all 
recommendations can be made for improving the use of rational and heuristic elements during 
executive decision-making in Qatar.  
4) Elbanna & Child in (2007), rational processes have positive relationship and considered a 
central part of strategic decision-making. Goll & Rasheed in (1997); Elbanna in (2006) agreed 
that there is empirical evidence which ensured that the relationship between the rationality and 
organisational outcomes through decision-making process is a positive relationship. Dean and 
Sharfmanin (1996) found a stronger relationship between rationality and decision effectiveness 
in unstable environments, rather than in stable ones.  Francis (2016) found that older managers 
tend to lean and use rational model than younger as a solution for meeting risks, and rational 
model should be a part of decision-making process that all managers and employees have to 
apply through conducting their jobs.  
5) According to Gigerenzer (2007); and Luan et al. (2019), the heuristics is considered useful for 
decision making process when managers do not have the time to decide and the competition 
with competitors needs quick reaction. Passer & Smith in (2004), using a heuristic model can 
be convenient to define the discrepancies between these rational strategies and actual human 
thought processes which make it an integral part of decision-making process. Hafenbrdl et al. 
(2016) discussed the effects of heuristic model on decision-making model through taking the 
moderating of context-specific experience, technology adoption, and consumer inattention, 
arguing that applying a heuristic model could be beneficial for making decisions when there is 
a need for a quick reaction and there is no time to use all the data and information depending 
on the competitive situation. In addition, this research also agreed that heuristic model can 
affect positively in decision making process but there are several main conditions to be 
discussed related to the volume of information, time, and speed of decision-making. 
Thus, the implications this research presents to the contemporary theories of Rational and 
Heuristic Elements in the decision-making process is that this has now been critically studied 
in the context of Executive Management in Qatar. Furthermore, the literature on executive 
management decision making is extended because of this thesis publication with a theoretical 
model of a decision-making process represented in Figure 5.1 which has been presented and 
discussed in the context of Qatar.  
This study proposed a new mixed environment driven model to decision making process 
based on research results and findings as depicted in Figure 5.3.  This model supports previous 
literature on decision making styles. Previous decision-making theorists used rational decision 
style and intuitive style as two separate techniques of making decisions. To face the challenges of 
the environment that decision-makers must deal with, the mixed approach is propriate. In this 
model, the more complex, dynamic, and poor environment the more intuition and heuristic, the less 
rational. On the other hand, the more simple, stable, and rich environment the more rational and 
avoidant, and the less intuition and heuristic.  
CHAPTER SIX: CONCLUSION 
6.1 INTRODUCTION  
The strategic environment of Qatar is dramatically changing since the blockade of 5th June 
2017. As a small country, Qatar had to face numerous challenges and threats created by the 
blockade and the countries involved, to include sustaining normal life to its citizens and working 
labour forces of expatriates and keeping the execution retheme of its mega projects of highways, 
bridges stadiums and all preparations to world cup in 2022, where urgent plans executed to find 
new lines of logistics to sustain operations of all these projects and to keep normal life. Thus, the 
great success in managing the crisis is attributed to the talented experienced staff and top 
executives who developed excellent plans using instruments of the state national power to include 
political, economic, military, and informational to face the threats that were imminent to happen 
and deal with the consequences. Accordingly, the nature of todays environment has the utmost 
complexity, dynamism accompanied with scarcity of resources which complicated decision-
making processes where good planning became a victim uncertainty. Incomplete and imperfect 
information a companied with scarcity of resources and time constraints are the dominant factors 
when taking decisions, besides what complicate decision process in terms of shifting, ill-defined 
or competing goals. People trying hard to figure out what to do in the face of such difficult 
circumstances. Activities involving decision making processes under these circumstances are 
complex, so governments struggle on daily bases facing such challenges when the stakes often 
high and the impact of decisions are significant on people lives.  
Optimal solutions to current problems need substantial efforts to be devoted to adopting 
the best mechanism to make decisions. Accordingly, people that are using their knowledge and 
experience in coping with complex decision tasks are expert decision makers, which does not mean 
decision analysts, but they are familiar with the tools and information sources relevant to deciding. 
6.2 RESEARCH PURPOSE AND AIM 
The research analysed in detail the decision-making processes and the use of rational, 
heuristics and four other factors that in total accounted for 77% of the variance in executives 
decision-making processes in Qatar.  The utility of this study and its contribution on individual-
difference decision-making paradigm in the academic research is related to the possibility of 
developing a wider and more comprehensive taxonomy of rational and heuristic decision-making 
process, still grounded on an evidence-based approach. Adopting evidence- based categories of 
rational and heuristic may allow future research to investigate more precisely the presence of 
multiple processes in decision-making against the idea of a single decision-making competence 
(Ceschi, Costantini, Phillips, & Sartori, 2017; Ceschi, Demerouti, Sartori, & Weller, 2017).  
On the other hand, even if the present study showed the presence of consistent relationships 
between some traits and rational and heuristic, some of the variance remains unexplained by 
leaving room to other possible predictors (expertise, tacit knowledge, and self-regulation mood) 
Which have proved to have a role in the Anchoring and Adjustment). Such variables, in 
comparison to individual differences, may be developable characteristics, which would be useful 
to train to avoid or apply certain rational or heuristic decision-making styles in specific contexts.  
6.3 RESEARCH REVIEW  
This research investigates the integration of rational and heuristic elements in executive 
decision-making processes in Qatar. Qualitative and quantitative research design is used in this 
study to address the research problem and research questions. Semi-structured interviews were 
conducted with four top executive decision makers, along with a research questionnaire using six 
decision-making styles identified from prior theorizing and empirical research and defined in 
behavioural terms according to Scott and Bruce (1995), Dew et al. (2009), Ballou (1989), and 
Williams (2010), that include rational, intuitive, dependent, avoidant, and spontaneous decision-
making style. Data were analysed using SPSS Version 23 using factor analysis, descriptive 
statistics, MANOVA and correlations.  
The results developed from the descriptive analysis of the average percentage to 
respondents responses (Agree and Strongly agree) of scale items of each factor taking separately, 
where 88.2% agree and strongly agree on rational decision-making style, 50.85% agree and 
strongly agree on intuitive decision-making style, 64.5% agree and strongly agree on dependent 
decision-making style, 20.76% agree and strongly agree on avoidant decision-making style, 95.8% 
agreement on expert heuristic decision-making style and 22.85% agreement on spontaneous 
decision-making style. What does that mean? From the total respondents 27.9% agree and strongly 
agree on expert heuristic decision-making style, 25.72% agree and strongly agree on rational 
decision-making style, 14.82% agree and strongly agree on intuitive decision-making style, 18.8% 
agree and strongly agree on dependent decision-making style, 6.66% agree and strongly agree on 
spontaneous decision-making style, and 6.1% agree and strongly agree on avoidant decision-
making style. These findings support the argument of Sadler-Smith (2005) that despite practicing 
one dominant style, people were likely to use various decision-making styles supporting 
previously published quantitative research findings published by Sadler-Smith (2005). It is also 
noticed that the correlations found in this study support the notion that the decision-making styles 
are not mutually exclusive and that individuals do not rely on a single decision-making style but a 
combination of decision-making styles in making important decisions, which support the argument 
of Scott & Bruce (1995), However, research indicates that individuals, due to a lack of time and 
commitment, tend to rely on shortcuts when making decisions (Payne et al., 1993). Consequently, 
the concept of fast and frugal heuristics has gained attention as an important concept to describe 
human judgments (Gigerenzer et al., 1999) which supports the findings of this study, where 
respondents mostly exhibited expert heuristic decision-making style with 95.8% (agree and 
strongly agree responses). 
 When factor analysis is used to reduce number of items to six components (concepts that 
group together in the respondents minds), the following findings were noticed: 
1) Factor 1  Q16, Q17, Q19 (Avoidant decision-making style) where avoidant decision-makers 
avoid making a decision whenever possible, the value of (p-value = 0.000), which means that 
the independent variables (Q16, Q17, Q19) together have a significant (substantial) effect on 
the dependent variable (Factor 1) at a significant level of (   0.05). 
2) Factor 2  Q33, Q34, Q38 (Heuristic decision-making style) where heuristic is a mental 
shortcut that allows people to solve problems and make judgments quickly and efficiently. 
These rule-of-thumb strategies shorten decision-making time and allow people to function 
without constantly stopping to think about their next course of action, the value of (p-value = 
.000), this means that the independent variables represented by (Q33, Q34, Q38) together have 
significant (substantial) effect on the dependent variable (Factor 2) at a level of significance of 
(  0.05).  
3) Factor 3  Q2, Q3, Q4 (Rational decision-making style) where rational decision-makers 
conduct an extensive search for alternatives and make a logical choice between them, the value 
of (p-value = 0.000), which means that the independent variables (Q2, Q3, Q4) combined have 
a significant (substantial) effect on the dependent variable (Factor 3) at a significant level of 
(  0.05) 
4) Factor 4  Q6, Q7 (Intuitive Decision-making Style) where intuitive decision-makers use their 
intuition to make decisions, the value of (p-value = 0.000). This means that the independent 
variables represented by (Q6, Q7) together have a significant (substantial) effect on the 
dependent variable (Factor4) at the significant level of (  0.05). 
5) Factor 5  Q13, Q15 (Dependent Decision-making Style) where dependent decision-makers 
rely on others to guide their decision-making, the value of (p-value = 0.000), which means that 
the independent variables represented by (Q13, Q15) combined have a significant (substantial) 
effect on the dependent variable (Factor5) at the level of Significance of (  0.05). 
6) Factor 6  Q21, Q22 (Spontaneous Decision-making Style) where decision makers made Quick 
and impulsive decisions), the value of (p-value = 0.000), which means that the independent 
variables represented by (Q21, Q22) combined have significant (substantial) effect on the 
dependent variable (Factor 6) at the level of Significance of (  0.05). 
The correlations found between different factors is supported by some of previous 
literature. A positive significant correlation of Factor 3 (Rational decision-making style) with 
Factor 4 (Intuitive decision-making style) at the 0.01 level, support the argument made by Betsch 
& Kunz (2008), that intuitive thinkers were more likely to make intuitive decisions and rational 
thinkers were more likely to make a rational decision.  
Avoidant decision-making style is characterized by attempts to avoid decision making. The 
negative significant correlation between Factor 1 (Avoidant decision-making style) and Factor 3 
(Rational decision-making style) at the 0.01 level supports the argument of Scott & Bruce (1995) 
and the conclusion of Phillips et al. (1984) that rational decision makers tend to approach rather 
than avoid problems. The avoidant decision-making style was found to be negatively related to 
well-being (Bavolr & Orosov, 2015), also depression was found to be positively correlated with 
the avoidant style, brooding and anxious style and was negatively associated with the vigilant and 
intuitive decision-making styles. In the few studies with employees as participants, Rational (Russ 
et al.,1996, sales-managers) and Intuitive (Sadler-Smith, 2004, managers) styles were found to be 
associated with better job performance and avoidant style (Russ et al.,1996) with poorer job 
performance. 
According to Allwood & Salo (2012), persons scoring high on dependent style have less 
flexibility in planning and accomplishing their decision-making errands because they are 
dependent on other persons in the decision process. This would result in less control over the 
decision process and its outcomes. Dependent styles suggests that dependence on others in 
decision-making can be accompanied by a lower tendency to avoid making decisions. Avoidance 
can be inevitable only with a lack of other people able to provide advice, while a higher reported 
use of the rational style correlated positively with the dependent style (Bavolr & Orosov, 2015), 
which concurs with the findings of two previous studies Baiocco et al. (2009 and Loo, (2000). A 
possible explanation could be that an effort to seek advice from other people is a part of a rational 
process (Bavolr & Orosov, 2015). 
Factor 3 (Rational decision-making style) has positive significant correlation with Factor 
4 (Intuitive Decision Making Style) at the 0.01 level, A classification scheme for decision-making 
style proposed by Harren (1979) includes two opposing categories in regard to information 
gathering and evaluation: intuitive and rational. Therefore, individuals can be classified as 
consistently intuitive or consistently analytical in information gathering and evaluation. However, 
optimal strategic decision making may require both rationality and intuition (Elbanna, 2006; 
Elbanna & Child, 2007; Hitt & Tyler, 1991; Langley et al.,1995). Intuition denotes a vague 
feeling or sense of feeling of pattern or relationships. It is also referred as holistic thinking, 
immediate insight, seeing the answer without knowing how it was reached (Thorne, 1990), or as 
a technique of swiftly retrieving hunks and forms of knowledge molded from previous 
experience (Seal, 1990). Decision-makers might sense patterns, feelings, and objects in seemingly 
unconnected facts. Intuition is often perceived by people as a sudden awareness of information 
(Ritter, & Dijskterhuis, 2014; Sauter, 1999). Intuitive managers become impatient with routine, 
details, or repetition. They may reach conclusions too quickly, ignore relevant facts, or follow an 
inspiration when it is clearly bad (Agor, 1984). The factors of intuitive decision-making style 
consist of experience, knowledge, and others (Harteis et al., 2008). 
A Mixed environment driven model to decision-making process is proposed based on 
research results and findings besides environment characteristics and setting that increase 
uncertainty as depicted in Figure 6.1.  This model supports previous literature on decision making 
styles, where previous theorists used rational decision style and intuitive style as two separate 
techniques of making decisions. A classification scheme for decision-making style proposed by 
Harren (1979) includes two opposing categories regarding information gathering and evaluation 
which are intuitive and rational. Therefore, individuals can be classified as consistently intuitive 
or consistently analytical in information gathering and evaluation. 
Figure 6.1 
Mixed Environment Driven Model to Decision-making Processes 
Three factors that can increase uncertainty in organisation environment. These factors are 
complexity, dynamism, and richness, As the environment becomes more complex, less stable, and 
poorer, the level of uncertainty increases. Environmental complexity is a function of the strength, 
number, and interconnectedness of the specific and general forces that an organisation must 
manage. The greater the number, and the greater the differences between them, the more complex 
and uncertain is the environment and the more difficult to predict and control (Child, 1972). 
Environmental dynamism is a function of how much and how quickly forces in the specific and 
general environments change over time and thus increase the uncertainty an organisation faces 
(Aldrich, 1979). Environmental richness is a function of the amount of resources available to 
support an organisations domain, where in rich environments, uncertainty is low because of the 
plethora of resources and the lack of need to compete for them (Starbuck, 1976). The certainty is 
high in simple, stable, and rich environment, but in complex, dynamic and poor environment the 
uncertainty is high.  
When analyzing this type of environment where uncertainty is the concern for decision 
makers during decision making processes, it is concluded that using one decision-making style 
will be costly whether in preparation phase or as consequence of a wrong decision, accordingly, 
and to reach an effective decision a mixed environment driven model to decision-making process 
is proposed.  
Rational Decision-Making Style is characterized by a thorough search for and logical 
evaluation of alternatives (Scott & Bruce, 1995), where Rational causes decision makers to be 
obliged to consider several alternative scenarios and probabilities for each alternative before 
making decision (Busari & Spicer, 2015; Oliveira, 2007). According to Dean & Sharfman (1993), 
rationality is the extent to which the decision-making process involves the collection of 
information relevant to the decision, and the reliance upon analysis of this information in making 
the choice. Rational decision-making incorporates critical evaluation of evidence and a structured 
process that requires time and conscious effort (Fitzgerald et al., 2017). Rational procedures help 
decision makers establish relevant decision criteria, identify a comprehensive set of alternatives, 
and evaluate the individual alternatives objectively (Kaufmann et al., 2012). In contrast to intuitive 
decision-making style, rational decision-making style encompass cautious and methodical thought 
fulness of all conceivable decision choices (Baird, 1989; Tetlock et al.,1993). Scrutinizing the 
available options by means of rational decision-making style necessitates the decision maker to 
deliberately appraise options and their relevant probable consequences. In addition to being a 
cognizant and thoughtful process, rational decision-making style is largely free from 
ridiculousness and predispositions, and thus results in enhanced decision (Pacini & Epstein, 1999), 
higher work and organisational performance (Busari & Spicer, 2015; Singh, 2014; Smolka et al., 
2018; Uzelac et al., 2016). 
Rational economic model based on neo-classical economic ideology was also governed by 
logic and rational analysis in decision scenarios (Hendry, 2000). All the options are considered, 
rationally evaluated, and the final decision is based on optimal choices (Hellreigel et al., 2001; 
Mangalindan, 2004). The perfect rationalism was challenged with the passage of time. Simon 
(1957) criticized the mean-end sequence and proposed a bounded rational model which is well 
known as normative or administrative model of decision making. Thus, the perfect rationality 
turned into bounded rationality based on limited information processing, judgment heuristics, and 
satisfying choices. later, Lindbloms (1959) incremental model was also based on limited rationality 
although these models were more flexible than perfect rationalism. The major transition took place 
when Kahneman & Tversky (1979) rejected the legacy of perfect rationality and proposed prospect 
theory of decision making. Time constraints, decision speed, and cost of acquiring information 
restricted theorists to make decisions with an adequate amount of information (Buchanan & 
Connell, 2006), therefore decision makers tried to find alternatives to rational theory and this why 
intuitive decision-making style earned much popularity in the recent years. While theorists limited 
decision-making to either rational or intuitive strategies, Andersen (2000) proposed that 
individuals decision-making styles can be identified by assessing these two functional dimensions 
in conjunction. 
Decision literature depicts that past researchers introduced numerous unique decision-
making styles according to their cultures, contexts, and circumstances, but the shared aspect of the 
research on decision making styles is the consideration of rational and intuitive decision-making style 
by almost all of the decision researchers (Arroba, 1977; Harren, 1979; Mitroff & Kilmann, 1975; 
Phillips et al.,1984; Scott & Bruce, 1995) although they considered both styles as distinctive 
dimensions of human nature. Researchers (Klaczynski, 2001; Stanovich & West, 2000; Sternberg, 
1997) illustrated that the line of distinction between cognitive style, learning style, thinking style, 
and decision-making style is based on the distinction between rational and intuitive approaches. 
The past literature indicates that decision researchers were stick to the assumption that decisions 
are solely based either on rationality or on intuition. Payne & Bettman (2004) suggested that 
attempts should be made to understand the interaction between these two styles instead of 
considering them as separate dimensions to confirm Stanovich & West, (2002) argument that these 
two styles do not stand apart on the poles and are more interactive in nature. The mixed 
environment driven model to decision making process, this research proposed came to support 
Rowe & Boulgarides (1992) argument that the distinction and integration in rational and intuitive 
decision-making style are based on individual differences, organisational, contextual, and 
environmental factors, but in a broader prospect that include other decision-making styles, 
heuristic, dependent and avoidant. In todays environment organisations are forced to work under 
uncertainty, where multicultural and diversity of working forces, environment is the dominant and 
the room for individual differences expanded. In the times of emergency, complexity, ambiguity, 
uncertainty, risk, and time pressure, intuitive decision-making style is  more appropriate that involves 
a creative solution based on overall situational recognition instead of making a feature-by-feature 
analysis (Bryant, 2002; Callan & Proctor, 2000), Similarly, in the absence of the consensus and in 
the presence of uncertainty, rational style is inappropriate (Dean & Sharfman, 1993), but  Bass & 
Reggio (2006) suggested that both styles stands on a continuum on which an individual leader
